## THE POST-MEDIUM CONDITION by Michael Archer

A very different exploration of the relationship between lookg and beig loke at can be seenn the orkoe Canadian Stan Douglas (b.96). Two early series of short pieces - Television Spots (1987-88) and Monodramas (1991) the dience' nderstanding f the tandardf with them. A woman walks through an underground car park and stops momentarily to adjust her shoe strap. Does the camera that tracks her movement cast us in the role of voyeur or predator, or are we merely the observers of a mundane action? A car and a school bus meet at a road junction, their awkward manoeuvres in attempting to pass each other heightened by the fact that the bus appears to be on the wrong side of the road. A man greets another by name, only for him to turn and say,'I'm not Gary'. Since the two men are close there can be no possibility of misrecognition, so we are forced to ask ourselves what significance this person's refusal to accept the name could mean. As Douglas has said, 'The doubt, that pronounal doubt, doubt of pronouns, doubt of the certainty of an I, is the a priori of my work.' The man who greets 'Gary' is white, the one who is 'not Gary' is black, and in his denial lies the rejection of all those stereotypical assumptions that would ignore his personal qualities and instead draw generalized and offensive ethnic distinctions.

The very active part the viewer plays in constructing the identities of others is experienced in Hors Champs (1992) (,). For this, Douglas filmed a session involving three musicians associated with the free jazz movement of the late 196os who had found greater expressive freedom for their music in Paris than their native US. The presence of those musicians in France, 'may be considered continuous with the history of black American musicians emigrating to France, which extends back at least as far as the arrival of Josephine Baker and Sydney Bechet on European soil'. Filmed in black and white using two cameras, and on a set reminiscent of French TV programs of the time, Douglas

Stan DouglasHors Champs,.Two-channel video projecion c whie. Isallain iCLnon Dvwi Galle Ne edited a master tape that focused in the usual way on whoever was soloing or taking the musical lead at any one moment. Alongside this he edited together what remained to make another tape of out-takes' in which we see people waiting their turn, listening, looking around and so on. These two videos are projected from opposite corners of a room onto either side of a large, double-sided screen suspended diagonally across the centre of the space. It is therefore impossible to watch both versions of this event simultaneously. Instead, the viewer has to make a choice either to watch the authorised version, or to see, 'a counternarrative of everything that had been edited out'. In addition to its associations with the civil rights movement of the 196os, jazz is also significant in Douglas's work as a critique of the analysis of Modernism made by the German sociologist and critic Theodor Adorno (1903-1969). In particular, Adorno's championing of the music of Schoenberg was done at the expense of jazz, which he considered to be wholly subject to the dominant forces of industrialised society and of little aesthetic value. As Douglas's work leads us to acknowledge, what this effectively did was to marginalise black people, to exclude them from the history of modernism, and to suppress acknowledgement of their contributions to cultural life.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/192c5917c09747a1b4da512bdb0033f73a0a4aa1bda5f79fe7b4d70234f1626e.jpg)

Douglas again used double projection for Le Détroit (1999-2ooo), this time projecting positive and negative versions of the same short loop onto either side of a translucent screen to produce a solarised effect (,36). Once again, a technique which has a very particular place within the history of modernism, both in the earlier solarised photographs of Man Ray, and the doubling of positive and negative images used by László Moholy-Nagy, a process which in its turn influenced Andy Warhol, is taken by Douglas and invested with further socio-political significance. The loop shows a woman warily observing a house from a nondescript car - possibly an unmarked police car? - before deciding to go in to investigate. As she moves through the abandoned building she makes small efforts to re-establish some sort of order, straightening furniture, picking up pieces of paper, and so on. Upstairs she is disturbed by something and runs back to the car, knocking the furniture and brushing the paper back on to the floor on the way. Before driving off she again observes the house and, once more, decides to go in to investigate. All the while, the positive and negative versions of the loop interfere with one another,creating small disturbances in the image which hold out the hope that it will either fully viwer wuld therey beble to ecpe fom able cycle. This never happens, of course, and we remain trapped within its repetitions, sharing both the anxieties a the curiosities of the protagonist.All these pies, Douglas has said of Le Détroit and other recent works, 'reintroduce fear, which has been taken away by modernity: a lot of the promise of modernity was of a lie without fear, violence, throug rational government and so on.That far is just being repressed by institutional mechanisms.'

896 Google Digitized by

Original from UNIVERSITY OF VIRGINIA

Although it is conceived as art and, with the exception of the Television Spots which were originally made for TV, shown in art galleries, Douglas's work has nonetheless made frequent reference to the fact that video art shares not only its technology with TV, but also much of its associated way of seeing. The American writer Rosalind Krauss has spoken of the impact of video on art making as something which itself 'shattered the modernist dream'. Quite unlike painting, sculpture or even photography, each of which can be thought of as a medium because they act as physical embodiment of, or support for works of art, video, insofar as it is technologically equivalent to TV, 'splinters spatial continuity into remote sites of transmission and reception.' In other words, with video we

Stan Douglas, Le Detroit, 1999-2ooo. 35mm film installation for two 35mm film projectors, looping device anamorphic lens, one screen, stills from video. David Zwirner Gallery, New York.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/938de788e1b0c137045af13a830788040aad7cc508abdac58858beb8fe5e8a5a.jpg)

are no longer able to identify just where it is that the work of art is that we are looking at. It is potentially both everywhere and nowhere, and because of this the modernist idea that the power of a work of art lay in the tension between the world it represented and the materials it shaped and formed in the making of that representation lost its power. The 'post-medium condition' that Krauss suggests art has now entered, differs from the way in which Post-Modernism was understood to challenge Modernism. The pluralism and hybridization spoken of at the beginning of this chapter, while signalling a radical change in the cultural field, could still be thought of in opposition to the established categories of painting and sculpture, and therefore in a certain sense remained dependent on them. Appropriation, dispersal, impermanence, and so on, still drew their strength and meaning as provocative tactics from the defining status of the authentic, autonomous art object that they desired to subvert. Similarly, video was largely taken to be another medium to set alongside and interfere with the traditional ones. Krauss's point is that video should not be thought of in this way. The implication is that there is no longer a need to rely, either directly or, as with Post-Modernism, ironically on the notion that a particular medium was a necessary and constitutive part of the aesthetic experience. Thus, while all and every means of representation are available to the contemporary artist - including, of course, painting and sculpture - he or she is now able to make use of them without feeling an obligation before all else to comment upon the way in which they have been exploited before.

These qualities of video - its ability to mimic the mind rather than the eye, to reveal patterns of thought and behaviour, to expose and dissect social and political realities, to cause us to reflect upon our ways of seeing and understanding the world - have all contributed to a revitalised use of narrative, a development that is very clear in the work of the American artist Matthew Barney (b. 1967). At Documenta IX in 1992, Barney exhibited OTTOshaft in a room on the lower level of Kassel's central underground car park. The video had been shot in the same location and was installed together with several of the strange props used in its making. In the film we see a sequence of visually compelling, but not always entirely comprehensible scenes. Barney climbs naked up the car park's elevator shaft; figures dressed in kilts and carrying bagpipes drive down the entrance ramp attempting, it seems, to accomplish a task that is never quite completed; a rolled up rubber mat  the one on the floor next to us as we watch the screen - is dropped and a figure falls out of it; other props appear, plugged into various bodily orifices. The OTTO of the title is a reference to the footballer Jim Otto, whose name has formal significance for Barney, the two Os standing for bodily orifices and the Ts, the uprights of the goalposts. He says, though, that 'forms don't really take on life for me until they've been 'eaten', passed through the narrative construction.' The physicality of the human body, and the way in which the story told by the work is intimately bound up with the body's workings is of great

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/f224e6cd4ad995906692f73d06d222a324586c89f7da9e42a7ae81b0c4202d98.jpg)

,37 Matthew Barney, Cremaster 2, 19gg. High definition television in colour, transferred to 35mm, 77 minutes, production still. Barbara Gladstone Gallery, New York.

importance here. A simple distinction between male and female is inadequate. What Barney creates instead is a field of activity which might have many 'different zones of sexual articulation'. One of his notes for the work states OTTOshaft: hypoxia→ hypertrophy hubris: POUNDCAKE', a progression that he subsequently explained thus: 'In OTTOshaft, Otto and Al Davis try to take this glucose pill through the metabolic change from glucose to sucrose to candy to petroleum jelly to tapioca to meringue and then to pound cake. If they could just get it to pound cake - its state of hubris - then the bagpipe would play 'Amazing Grace'. But it never gets there; it gets trapped in meringue.'

Such an explanation might seem to obscure far more than it elucidates, but the visually inventive power of Barney's work transforms his personal myth-making into work that charts our more general struggle to establish a sense of identity. The ideas in OTTOshaft were carried over into Barney's next major project, a series of five films with the overall title Cremaster. (The cremaster is the muscle in the male body that controls the descending and ascending of the testicles.) The first of the series to be filmed, Cremaster 4 (1994), is set on the Isle of Man. Two motorcycles with sidecars race round the island in opposite directions. One is blue, symbolising descent, and the other yellow for ascent. Barney plays a character called 'The Loughton Candidate', named after a ram indigenous to the island which is unique in having two sets of horns, one ascending and the other descending. The Loughton color, of course, is green, a combination of blue and yellow, and we thus see in the film an attempt by Barney to realise this doubled state, of both/and rather than either/or, for himself. Cremaster 1 (1995), with references to Busby Berkeley-style choreography, is set in a stadium in Idaho, Cremaster 5 (1997), starring Ursula Andress as The Queen of Chain, takes place in the Opera House and Gellert Baths in Budapest, and Cremaster 2 (1999), in which Barney plays the executed murderer Gary Gilmore, ranges from a glacier to the Utah salt flats (,. As the British writer Polly Staple has written of Cremaster : 'It is tempting to get caught up in describing the details, or in attempting to decipher the meaning of Cremaster 2 but this seems unnecessarily laboured as the film's exploration of the driving forces of masculinity, the defiance of physical limitations and the potential and the curtailment of individual freedom works through a system of fragile sculptural layering of sound and visual effect, pace and tension, light and color, visceral liquid and contemplative atmosphere.' Barney's work is difficult, but its diffculty is a challenge to the blandishments of a culture that would have us always take the line of least resistance.

## PHOTOGRAPHY AND THECONSTRUCTION OF REALITY

Several of the German photographers who came to prominence during the 198os had studied at the Düsseldorf Academy under Bernhard and Hilla Becher, and the clear, objective approach taken by the Bechers in their photographs of pit head machinery, water towers, steelworks and other industrial installations (22.6) - the so-called Anonymous Sculptures' - is clearly visible in the photographs of these younger artists. As the critic Alex Alberro has said, Although each photographer is remarkable in his or her own way, they are unified by an easily recognisable style that privileges meticulously composed scenes produced with the highest possible definition and tonal differentiation.' In some respects, the cool objectivity found in the work of Thomas Ruff (1958), Thomas Struth (b. 1954), Candida Höfer (b. 1944), Andreas Gursky (b. 1955) and others, can be seen as continuing in the tradition of the German documentary photography of the inter-war years. In that earlier work, the emphatic realism of a scene was recognisable both in the specificity that unique scene to reveal the more general social and mic orces at play within  Likewise in the wrk this new generation of artists we can see, for example, that the interior shot of an institution such as a museum, zoo, or university, indicates not only the simple function of the building, but also the cultural beliefs and assumptions that underpin s existence by leading the ye to details such as the way in which specimens are displayed, choice and arrangement of furniture, architectural style and so on. How w e  e relation  he nimals to us humans i what r knowledge we are able to accumulate by turning our curiosity nto such a world, and what all of this tells us by such images.

Moreover, whether their subject is a person, a building, a street scene or the night sky, the detached manner of presentation in this work emphasises the fact that it is as much the way in which the camera functions that emotional characteristics of a scene, as it is our own eyes.

An image of the night sky, for example, shows an enormous number of stars that, in the normal way, would be invisible to the naked eye. Similarly, in many other mundane instances it is the camera itself that enables us to see those things which, while always there, usually pass unnoticed or remain unseen. In his 1936 essay, "The Work of Art in the Age of Mechanical Reproduction', the German writer Walter Benjamin had referred to this important capacity of photography as a kind of optical unconscious: 'The act of reaching for a lighter or a spoon is familiar routine, yet we hardly know what really goes on between hand and metal, not to mention how this fluctuates with our moods. Here the camera intervenes with the resources of its lowerings and liftings, its interruptions and isolations, its extensions and accelerations, its enlargements and reductions. The camera introduces us to unconscious optics as does psychoanalysis to unconscious impulses.'

Thomas Struth's views of city streets showed them not so much deserted as unpopulated. Through this absence of people, one is able to look at these 'Unconscious Places' with a dispassionate eye and to see revealed in the buildings, their style, arrangement, and state of repair, the festoons of phone lines, the clutter of TV aerials, and so on, not only the physical reality of a particular urban location, but also the logic of their planning and the ideas, attitudes and habits of their inhabitants. 'When you compare my photograph of Rue Saint-Antoine in Paris with other photographs of streets taken in New York or in Germany, you can see their exemplary value for a certain historical or architectural proposition or typology. If the scenes are empty, the effect is stronger, and when you look at these empty streets you can more easily imagine yourself in the space.' In another series of works, Struth addressed the activity of looking from another perspective, this time

photographing visitors in the act of contemplating paintings in the world's famous galleries and museums. What the people in the photograph are doing as they look, for example, at J.-L. David's Coronation of Napoleon in the Louvre is similar to what we, too, do as we stand in a gallery looking at Struth's image of them (,). Acknowledging this should lead us, as Struth says, to recognise that the works are not merely fetishes of our consumer society, and that the act of looking can instead be a trigger to our own understanding and to our engagement with history.

After early photographs of people enjoying their leisure time in the countryside, in which he made conscious reference to the German Romantic tradition and to painters such as Caspar David Friedrich, Andreas Gursky's work shifted in focus and scale in the 199os. While leisure still features  the huge May Day IV from 2ooo, 6ft 6ins x 16ft 5ins (1.98 x 5m), for example, shows hundreds of clubbers at a May Day rave  other aspects of human activity are equally to the fore. Three works from the early 199os, for instance, show the following: hundreds of cars line up neatly on the dockside at Salerno waiting for shipment while the buildings of the town recede into the mountainous landscape beyond the bay in the far distance; buses, cars, bikes and people meet chaotically at a junction in Cairo while business carries on as normal in the surrounding streets; and in a large, neon-lit production workshop at the Siemens factory in Karlsruhe, workers sit at rows of benches whose surfaces are neatly stocked with reels of cable and boxes of electronic components (n,). A line of trollies laden with more cable and equipment stands in the foreground, while myriad power leads spiral down from the ceiling. In each of these instances, as in many of Gursky's images, the scenes are photographed

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/1ceda9a7b23bd3fc25bc83f70a6844e3a1aa7801a07e8d2290417433407969bf.jpg)

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/39c3e94822afa88e9e76e157a54d354f61f3c11a852af9845ae826d8cbf202e5.jpg)

fully to comprehend their overwhelming complexity, to make it manageable both for him and for us. 'My u  u desire, perhaps illusory, to keep track of things and maintain my grip on the world.' What these images lead us to concentrate on, therefore, is not so much the specific site being photographed, but the way in which it is able to represent a larger system of human exchange, such as trade, commerce, industry and so on, and of the political and social relations that sustain those systems. Similarly, the many tiny figures in Gursky's images can be seen less as specific personalities than as representatives of people as a whole: 'The camera's enormous distance from these figures means that they become de-individualised. So I am never interested in the individual, but in the human species and its environment.'

Traditionally, photography has been able to represent the world convincingly through its ability to reproduce the kind of perspectivally coherent space with which we have been familiar since the Renaissance. Gursky's work shows us that it is becoming increasingly difficult to rely on that ability. The forest of power cables snaking down from the ceiling in Karlsruhe together with the bright spots of the many primary colored objects scattered around the factory all catch the eye and cause it to roam over the surface of the image rather than to look into the depths of the depicted spae, sewhat as i we wee loking at a Jckson Pollk Gr reliant wholly on the camera, but also makes use of computer technology. In many cases he digitally enan certain areas f theagein rder to heihten te clr contrast. Chicago Board of Trade Il is a clear example of where this has been done, the different colored jackets of the traders tanding out vivily against the ta of the trading floor where they stand amidst the litter of discarded buy and sell orders (n,). Gursky has also double exposed certain sections of the scene, blurring some of the figures in order to emphasise the noise, bustle and overall sense of controlled chaos within the hall. Confronted with such an image, the viewer finds it hard to orient the eye in relation to any focal point since the intervention of the computer, which in scanning an image gives equal attention to each and every point on its surface, introduces a radically different means of spatial representation to that of one-point perspective. Through our inability to fix upon the centre of Gursky's huge image we are made to recognise that the financial activity it shows is something that spreads everywhere, that it i,indeed, a global phenomenon.

Gursky has also used the computer on other occasions to construct works by collaging together several separately photographed elements. Untitled V (1997), for example, a vast display of trainers indicating the ubiquity of this emblematic item of our consumer society, was made by combining six shots of a smaller shelving unit restocked each time. Another artist who has employed computer technology in the construction of his painstakingly choreographed images is the Canadian Jeff Wall (b. 1946). His The Flooded Grave shows a cemetery in his home city of Vancouver on an overcast day. In the foreground of the pu h   v which can be seen a rich variety of sea life (n,). It is a dream-like image, which Wall describes thus:'The viewer might imagine walking on a rainy day. He or she stops before a flooded hole and gazes into it and for some reason imagines the ocean bottom. We see the instant of that fantasy, and in another instant it will be gone.' The idea that photography is able to show us reality in a straightforward way is immediately compromised by a work such as this. It may be that out of habit we can accept this image at face value on first seeing it, but it quickly becomes evident that it is a very deliberate and complex composition. A red hose laid across the grass to the right is set against a coiled blue hose to the left. The handle of a spade stuck into the ground next to the grave leads the eye up to a grave monument behind and then to a tall tree in the background. A pick-axe handle breaks into the picture in the extreme foreground. The strong diagonal formed by the protective board placed at the grave edge contrasts with the opposing line formed by the red hose. And so on. Even the positions of the birds in the sky and on the ground contribute to the formal rhythms of the overall composition. Much as we can analyse a painting by Poussin as a means to understanding how its formal organization contributes to the overall meaning, so in Wall's case the placement of and relationships between the different elements of his compositions build a dense web of references and allusions. His work sits in the tradition of history painting, and in its treatment of reality and fantasy, of life and death, of belief, hope and wonder, The Flooded Grave calls to mind Courbet's A Burial at Ornans (15.38). Although it appears to be a seamless image, the work was actually put together from three distinct photographs. The background was shot in one cemetery, the foreground with hole in a second, and the sea-life  in a tank made from a cast of the hole  in Wall's studio. Each has its own point of view and, in a manner similar to that encountered in Cézanne's still life paintings, the eye shifts and slips from one to the other as it moves over the scene. What Wall's work reveals to us is that we can no longer treat a photograph as evidence of what

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/4b9c5a472ae9a2817afe5c5ccd954f96c9f6e9480b7145aff1c3e05604ebcb05.jpg)  
Andreas Gursky, Chicago Board of Trade II. 1999. C-print, 6ft gins x 1ft 5%/8ins(2.06x3.48m). Matthew Marks Gallery New York.

Far from being a simple transmission of facts about how things actually are, the pho u ha ishillly, misleadi an sheer pretnce.Before potographyys Wall, the coexistence of separate domains in pictures was taken for granted. Paintings showed angels or demons interacting with humans, for example, as a routine matter, because it is routine within the nature of the medium. Painting and drawing make no demand as to the ontological consistency of the things being depicted; they don't have any means to do so, and that's one of the main reasons they've been so significant in the history of the imagination. Photography seemed to be something quite different, at the beginning; it seemed to prove that there was only one world, not many - one visible world, anyway. But I think that is only a suggestion made by photography, not a conclusion. And the suggestion can be taken in so

Jeff Wall, The Flooded Grave, 1998-2ooo. Cibachrome transparency, aluminium light box, 7ft 6ins x 9ft 3ins (2.29 x 2.82m). Marian Goodman Gallery, New York.

many different ways. I think photography, by nature, does have artistically legitimate routes of access into the aesthetic of "multiple worlds".

Photography is used to trace out a narrative in the work of the Australian Tracey Moffatt (b. 1960). Moffatt is also a film maker, and when using still photography she invariably suggests possible story lines across a sequence of images rather than within a single isolated frame. Although they depict events of one sort or another, these sequences, however, do not so much tell a story as mark out a space within which many individual stories may occur, each shaped or constrained in its own way by the factors of gender, religious belief, inter-generational conflict, economic status, ethnic and sexual identity to which Moffatt alludes. The 1994 series, Scarred for Life, consists of nine panels, each of which contains an image together with a title, a date and a brief descriptive text. In'Birth Certifite, for example, a it dejectedlyhi a piece of paper and leaning her head against a sink. The caption reads: 'During the fight, her mother threw her birth certificate at her. This is how she found out her real father's name." Job Hunt, 1976' has a teenage boy, collar undone and tie loosened, standing against the outside wall of a house above the caption, 'After three weeks he still couldn't find a job. His mother said to him,"Maybe you're not good enough". The people in these images are both Aborigine, and part of the subtext to the stories is that there may have been some discrimination involved. Yet Moffatt does not allow the viewer to understand this in any straightforward way. Whereas in 'Job Hunt' there is a sense that perhaps the mother has internalised a sense of inferiority which she is now passing on to her son, the revelation of the gir' true to the building of a more assured sense of self out of the family conflict. In both cases, the issue of how ideas and values are transmitted from one generation to the other, and of how they are inevitably warped, misrepresented and y

Up in the Sky (1997) contains twenty five images; the majority are black and white, the remainder sepia toned (). It begins with a white mother holding an Aborigine baby in a bedroom through the window of which three nuns can be seen. The plasterboard in the room has been bashed in suggesting that exchange between the protective interior and what lies outside will happen sooner rather than later. An old man appears in the second image, crawling across the road in front of a 4 x 4, and these three figures childhoo, adulthood and old age cro up throughout the sequence. Three young men vent their anger by smashing up wrecked and abandoned cars, nuns take hold of the baby and hold it triumphantly aloft, people line a street as if waiting for a procession of some kind, and in the central quartet of sepia images, two men, seen from above, wrestle on the ground. It is a fight between good and evil, a struggle to assert and achieve some goodness in the world, though not necessarily goodness as understood by the nuns who have been so happy to reach and hold the infant. Later, one of the men lies, exhausted or injured, looking upward, his blank stare contrasting with the baby lying peacefully asleep on the bed. Ultimately, the narrative to which Up in the Sky introduces the viewer is that of life. Moffatt's characters, seen from a range of perspectives that are to a certain extent represented quite literally through the camera positions and the lighting chosen for the different shots - close-up, long shot, panorama, shot from above, from below, and so on - can be seen as symbolic of the larger forces that shape our lives. But they are also the individuals upon whom those forces act, and who strive not to be swept away by them.

## Abjection

One of the critical terms that gained currency during the 1990s was that of abjection. Linked to the notion that l  l to escape the workings of an economic system that positions us all as consumers, abjection stands as the only, very bleak alternative. Rather than the usual relationship involved in consumption - that of a consuming subject to a consumed object - abjection negates the possibility of abject. One artist to whose work this label was applied, despite his unease at its often glib usage, was the American Mike Kelley (b. 1954). A word he has much more readily used in connection with his own work is failure. Whatever we yearn for, whatever we wish to communicate, whatever we set out to achieve, our efforts will almost certainly end in glorious disaster. Inhis performances, dawigs vdeos, banners and installations of the 1980s and 1990s, Kelley has explored both failure itself, and the means, both subtle and disillusion and disappointment. One of a seris of banners Kelley made in the late 1980s, adopting the style made famous by the nun Sister Corita Kent, read: 'I Am Useless To The Culture But God Loves Me.' Such a knowledge may provie comfort, but it is,implies Kelley, a debilitag

comfort, one that justifies non-productivity, lack of manation an sol dysuncton:I thi we'e livi in a period in which victim culture and trauma are the rationale for everything. Especially in pop psychology, childhood trauma is the motivation behind every action.'

The starting point for Kelley's multi-part installation, Extracurricular Activity Projective Reconstruction #1 (A Domestic Scene), was a photograph seen in a high school yearbook (22,). The work is the third in a projected series of 365, one for each day of the year, the remainder of which will derive from similar source material: 'I wanted something very ritualised but very mundane. I also just needed a stockpile. It could just as easily have been movie stills. But I liked the yearbook pictures because they're so mundane. I only picked the ones that were extremely carnivalesque, that weren't normative. I didn't pick sports unless they were wacky sports. I chose artsy stuff or Dress-Up Day or hazing rituals. Pictures where, when you look at them, you don't know what's going on. You just know that it's this kind of free moment in an authoritarian system  a moment that transgresses the boundaries but that's completely allowed, even sanctioned by the system.' The image for EAPR #1 shows a moment from a production of an unnamed play in which two characters, one shabbily dressed, the other in smarter clothes, stand in a dingy bedsit. Around this photograph Kelley wrote a one act play in which the scruffily dressed man wakes up in the apartment but has no idea how or why he is there, his companion explains that they are lovers, and after a melodramatic exchange stuffed with precious references to Abstract Expressionist art, the Theatre of Cruelty and the poetry of Sylvia Plath, and many overwrought soliloquies, they agree upon a suicide pact, whereupon the two gas themselves in the oven that sits centre stage. 'What

interested me most was that the stage set made no sense. It has all the pieces from a bachelor apartment, but they're not composed in the right way. The stove's in the middle of the room, and there's a bed in front of the stove. So I immediately realised that the action in the play is built around the stove. That's what told me it was a suicide plot.'

EAPR #1 is made up of three elements: the yearbook photograph, a video of a performance of Kelley's script performed in suitably cod-expressionist style and filmed in a grainy black and white reminiscent of early live-theatre TV broadcasts, and a precise and finely crafted life-size reconstruction of the stage set. All the elements gesture towards the same reality, the original performance of the school play, and yet the truth of that reality remains unattainable. The different media employed in each one render them formally incommensurable, and the structural interdependence of each upon the other two sweeps the viewer up into a feverish loop from which it seems hard to escape. But the loop within which we are caught is as much cultural as it is narrowly artistic, in that the suicide in the pay  yk e her head in a gas oven. Plath, now, is an established part of the literary canon taught in schools. The original play, then, functions somewhat as a repressed memory that may surface unannounced in strange and fractured guise. By extension, we can understand that it is through art's attempts to represent what might have been that we are able to maintain any connection with our pasts.

A name often linked with Kelley's in discussion of the phenomenon of abjection, and an artist with whom he has collaborated in the past, is that of Paul McCarthy (b. 1945). In a string of performances, videos and wildly excessive installations made since the late 196os, McCarthy has repeatedly questioned society's moral precepts and

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/fda89ce0aa4a446cf2a04690c78a0611d051c58dafa9becec301ebd9920d415f.jpg)  
Mike Kelley, Extracurricular Activity Projective Reconstruction #1 (A Domestic Scene), 200o. Production still. © Mike Kelley.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/8719327812b63c28f0f88af1db037cc0754cc88408582595c7266fe0f6cd1dcf.jpg)  
Paul McCarthy. Bossy Burger, 199. Performance photograph. Courtesy Paul McCarthy and Luhring Augustine, New York.

behavioural norms. Fuelled by an apparent disregard for all and any proprieties, his elaborately orchestrated scenarios involving joke store masks, toys and dolls, sausages and endless quantities of tomato ketchup investigate the manner in which all objects and materials down to the most lowly and insignificant are saturated with cultural significance and meanings. The world, as McCarthy's work mercilessly insists, engineers our perceptions of right and wrong not in agreement with absolute standards, but in accordance with what is most economically and socially actions that stem from an immediate urge to express primal emotions such as rage or desire are themselves socally coded and determied Nothing is innoent, nd notiis purey persnal, becue whatever we is iven mean y    te our property lone McCarthy' is a worldin which innce is an illusion, and whee evn the most mundane nd unremarkable items to be found i the average suburban family home speak of our subjugation to the dictates of an oppressive social reality. What is more, it is a reality that is constructed in and mediated through popular culture. We could read the tomato ketchup that gets splattered around in the mock television environment of Bossy Burger as representing blood (n,). If we do, however, we must also acknowledge that the death which comes with its spilling in TV shows, films and cartoons is equally fake, the painless outcome of a struggle between good and evil that has more to do with fairy tales and Disneyland than anything else.

## Function and Value

A very different meditation on the nature of things and the question of how they function in establishing and mirroring patterns of human relation is found in the varied projects of the Cuban-American Jorge Pardo (b. 1963). In response to an invitation to participate in the o-yearly exhibition of publicly-sited sculpture in Münster in 1997, he designed Pier (n2, 4). Constructed of Californian redwood the pier runs 4o metres out into the nearby lake, the Aasee, and ends in a stepped hexagonal platform, on top of which there is an open, hexagonal pavilion. Visitors can stroll along the pier, sit on the steps and look over the water to the city beyond. If they like, they can buy some cigarettes from the machine in the pavilion and smoke while they contemplate the view. What one gets in the end, then, is the opportunity to look at and consider a view, just the sort of experience that is traditionally expected of a work of art. It is a view, moreover, from an unexpected place, a place that would be inaccessible but for the existence of the pier. Yet the pier exists and functions as a pier whether or not one chooses to think of the experience in aesthetic terms. Other projects by Pardo have included Reading Room (1996), a C-shaped redwood table, canvascovered stools and special light fittings for the Museum Boijmans van Beuningen, Rotterdam; Sailboat (1997),

Jorge Pardo Pie 9. Münster, Germany. Mixed media, lo Installation view. Friederich Petzel Gallery New York.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/d391832f393106c6ba9276bf5471f1a780cfb23ea3b814532e675a05d9a1c571.jpg)

a customised yacht installed in the foyer of the Museum of Contemporary Art, Chicago; Rosa (1996), tables and and even the design of his own house in Los Angeles. Visitors to Pardo's exhibition at the Los Angeles Museum of Contemporary Art would be taken by bus up to Washington Heights to see the house under construction. As part of an exhibition at the Zürich Kunsthalle in 2000 Pardo undertook, in addition to placing a range of works in the all l vere  eli   rvi a on the autobahn just outside the city. Its newly painted orange exterior now provides a vivid contrast with the surrounding landscape. With such works Pardo goes beyond the need to choose between different ways of appreciating an object. The spectator is not called upon to either contemplate the table or sit at it, admire the café or , az eou    ists have ceased to be relevant. What Pardo is more concerned to do is to question what it is that might allow an object or a set of procedures to assert their identity as art. His work can be understood as sculpture in that he is interested to conceive objects for placement in space. Furthermore, it is not only the function of such objects, but also the consideration of how the formal solution to the functional imperative can be traced back through a complex history of example and influence, that is of relevance. The space in which Pardo's work exists, as the critic Kate Bush has pointed out, is as much mental as physical. It is a space filled with questions about how things relate to one another. On one occasion, for example, Pardo made an arrangement of table, chairs and other smaller items for a shon   art t tm thael idea, 'things placed in the corner and colored blue.' What he was doing could on one level be likened to what all the rest of us do when we think about our own domestic interiors; on another, one could see a connection to a predecessor such as the Conceptual artist Lawrence Weiner, whose early works included such statements as 'Common Steel Nails Driven into the Floor at Points Designated at Time of Installation.' Once again, this might describe someone laying floorboards, or it could equally, as Weiner allows the viewer to decide, be taken as something which could be realised as a work of art. Pardo shares this thoughtful approach to the interplay between usefulness and aesthetic value with others, notable among whom is the German Tobias Rehberger (b. 1966). Questioning the way in which aesthetic quality is recognized and transmitted within and across cultures he has, in Peué Seé a Faágck Sunday Paáe (Never on Sunday) for example, sketched from memory items of furniture considered to be classics from designers such as Alvar Aalto and Le Corbusier, and given the drawings to African carpenters to realise.'A lot of people think that I want to present design as fine art,' says Rehberger, 'that's not the case, even if my works have lots of references to the history of architecture and design.. I think these copies make us see the European originals with new eyes... The African chairs have ben produc afer my copi, whic in theselve

Digitized by Google already contain misinterpretations. Some people have seen this work as politically unpleasant with respect to African culture, but I can't see it like that. The work is about translation.'In keeping with their ambiguous functional/aesthetic status, many of the relevant reference points for Pardo's works can also be found in the field of architecture and design. His house, for example, can be seen in the light of Modernism's contribution to Los Angeles domestic architecture through the work of Frank Lloyd Wright, R.M. Schindler, Richard Neutra and others. Li  he    L Trade Fair, Pardo took as his starting point a chair designed by Frank Gehry, himself a figure who has been pre-eminent in the field not only of architecture and design in general, but also, and more pecifically, infurthering the dialogue between architecture and art.

## ARTSPACES

As has been mentioned above, Gehry had developed his architectural ideas not least through his friendships and familiarity with the work of several important contemporary artists including Richard Serra and Claes Oldenburg. It was, too, an encounter with an early brick sculpture by Carl Andre that Gehry sees as his introduction to the Post-Modern idea of depthlessness. Each brick of Andre's sculpture was equal to every other, and this equivalence defeated the very idea that there could be an oginal of which the rest were copies, a specii starting point from which everything else proceeded. Likewise, space could no longer be treated as a rationally ordered environment, extending out from a central viewing position within which objects, people and buildings were seen to relate according to a hierarchy of values. Instead, the world was an open jumble of materials, all of which were available for transformation into architecture. Gehry's Guggenheim Museum in Bilbao (22,46), opened in 1997, stands as one of the most significant contributions to museum architecture since the spiral of Frank Lloyd

Frank Gehry. Guggenheim Museum, Bilbao, Spain, 1997.  
![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/caba381f90dcbf59102982df72b0ce6aeaf7dfe67091df039e2e62efd2993d23.jpg)  
Original from UNIVERSITY OF VIRGINIA

Wright's New York Guggenheim of the 195os and the glass box of Ludwig Mies van der Rohe's 196os Neue Nationalgalerie in Berlin. Its cluster of curved titaniumclad forms, glass walls and masonry façades, house a series of galleries, some of them rectangular, but many others, including the huge main 42oft long fish-shaped space that dwarfs even Serra's large steel sculptures, more eccentric and idiosyncratic in form. The response of many observers on its opening was to suggest that artists would be stimulated to make art of a new kind as a response to the challenge of the building. In its radical profile the Bilbao Guggenheim has fast gained a reputation as a building of the digital age, and of being one that successfully fuses the practical demands of the contemporary urban environment with the fantasy structures most usually associated with Disneyland. It is true that in developing the specifications for the museum, Gehry made use of a computer-modelling program initially devised for the aerospace industry, but he stresses that such technology only becomes useful after the initial stages of modelling in paper, wood and card. Commissioned in conjunction with New York's Solomon R. Guggenheim Foundation, the gallery is a key element in the Basque regional administration's efforts to revitalise an area in industrial decline. Together with its new museum the city now has a business district designed by Cesar Pelli, a subway system - the 'Fosterito' - by Sir Norman Foster, and a new airport and river bridge by the Catalan architect Santiago Calatrava.

Although exceptional in its impact, the Bilbao Guggenheim is only one of the major museum and gallery projects of the 199os. The Dutchman Rem Koolhaas designed the Rotterdam KunstHAL (1992), the American Richard Meier created the Museu d'Arte Contemporani (MACBA) for Barcelona (1995), Daniel Libeskind designed the Jewish Museum in Berlin (1996), another American,

, Richard Meier, Museu d'Arte Contemporani (MACBA). Barcelona, Spain, 1995.

Steven Holl, created a new museum of contemporary art, KIASMA, for Helsinki (1998), and the Swiss partnership of Jacques Herzog and Pierre de Meuron undertook the conversion of a former power station on the banks of the Thames in London into Tate Modern, which opened in 2000. The different approaches taken by these architects represent a broad spectrum of attitudes both to the legacies of Modernism, and to the critical examination of its precepts undertaken in the name of Post-Modernism. Each brings together a sense of the contemporary with an understanding of tradition in a unique way, and demonstrates that whatever the art gallery may be, it has ceased to perform the subordinate role of providing a polite backdrop for the appreciation of art objects. Holl's KIASMA, as its name implies, is a crossing point: a main rectangular block merges with a second volume that cuts through it in a sweeping arc. The orientation of the building and the dynamics of its intersecting elements serve to place it both physically amongst the structures of the contemporary city, and historically, in that it acts as a connection between two masterpieces by earlier Finnish architects, Eero Saarinen's railway station to one side, and Alvar Aalto's Finlandia Hall to the other. The clean lines of Meier's work, coupled with his use of white set his work in a clear relation to the pioneering buildings of the International Modern Style between the wars, particularly to the Purism of Le Corbusier. The amoeba-like floor plan of the small tower standing in front of the main MACBA building, the pierced screen above the entrance, and the ramp zig-zagging up the atrium behind the glass façade are all reminiscent of Corbusier's Villa Savoye (see p. 838). Rising rg th , whip halhe of the building, and looking out onto the surrounding plaza that had been created for the museum by clearing away several tenement blocks, it becomes apparent that

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/6dcdc4bfca59dd34c8205e413d699d6aad42aa1926acb2139117da54c008de4f.jpg)

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/2f1659bb418278097d3e310792af1e44797d781259a04de94792d8d6045cb0f0.jpg)  
Jacques Herzog and Pierre de Meuron, interior view of the conversion of Bankside Power Station into Tate Modern, London, 2000.

experiencing the architecture as a reinterpretation of Purist principles is an essential part of the spectator's activity, and not just a tedious preliminary to looking at art (22,47). In contrast to Meier's recreated Purism, the work of Herzog (b. 1950) and de Meuron (b. 1950) is characterised by formal simplicity and by their attention to the specific qualities of materials, be it the copper cladding used on a Basel signal box, the unfaced stone of a house wall, or the original brickwork of Tate Modern's Bankside Power Station (2,). Such directness often leads to striking effects, such as in their building for the Sammlung Goetz in Munich (z2,49), which takes the form of a wooden box sandwiched between two layers of glass. The two floors of galleries are similar, each being a sequence of spaces lit by a strip window running along the top of the walls. Since the lower floor is sunk into the ground, its windows form the lowest level of the gallery's façade. Like MACBA, Tate Modern has an atrium that takes up a great proportion of the building's overall volume, although in this case it is the result of a decision to be faithful to history and to leave as

Jacques Herzog and Pierre de Meuron, exterior view of Sammlung Goetz, Munich, Germany, 1991.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/c9400ee40173f340fda6f2da302f77da4dc36c092fe0129b037a05988182d72e.jpg)  
Digitized by Google

hear heliurig s Oi however, the building itself becomes an object of attention, and along with the picture windows giving a view of the city of London to one side of the galleries, there are other rest and observation areas which look into the turbine hall on the other.

Although the wide, single story entrance façade of Rotterdam's KunstHAL is clean and uncluttered, it gives access to a rich and complex space (z2,5o). The office set up by Rem Koolhaas (b. 1944) was given the name Office for Metropolitan Architecture (OMA), later expanded to OMA/AMO. The meaningless reversal (AMO) of the original name intended to indicate that Koolhaas's interest lies as much in research, investigation and the critical deconstruction of all architectural attitudes and assumptions as it does in producing individual buildings. The glass walls, floors and ceilings that are a feature of the KunstHAL can therefore be seen both as a reference to the glass entrance hall of Mies's Neue Nationalgalerie, and as an effort to dissolve the brute objectivity of the space into layers of translucency and transparency. The columns supporting the ground floor gallery are concrete cast to imitate tree trunks, while the lighting, rather than the usual carefully angled spotlights, is provided by a seemingly random arrangement of standard neon tubes.

Rem Koolhaas, interior view of KunstHAL, Rotterdam, Holland, 1992.  
![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/79b441e52ce454b6306152d55477fa53f1b41788bedd8035f8ae96a2cc1f29d2.jpg)

# Berlin and its International Building Exhibitions

2,5 Frankfurter-Allee, Berlin, completed 1960.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/0dd1509a66baff25677d18b6e8ca1f894640ebfa4769bce64c3dc5830329a5b2.jpg)

Between the early nineteenth century and the mid-twentieth, nearly all current systems of urban development were adopted in Berlin. An imposing group of museums was built near the royal palace and cathedral. Some of the first public parks were laid out. Terraces, squares and leafy suburbs provided residences for the middle classes. Factories, which include monuments of modern architecture (19.16) were kept to the outskirts with planned estates for workers nearby. Finally Hitler's architect Albert Speer (1905-1981) dreamed up a megalomaniac scheme only shortly before the whole city was devastated in the war. Destruction of some 50% of the housing then created the need and presented an occasion for vast schemes of reconstruction and Berlin became a laboratory of successive ideals of urbanism.

The Communist government of the eastern sector began in 1949 by commissioning the reconstruction of e   alo Frankfurter-Allee leading out of the city. It was widened to nearly 100 yards (9om) flanked by apartment blocks of eight to ten stories punctuated at about one third of its length by twin towers capped with cupolas similar to those of eighteenth-century buildings in Berlin .Two identical high-rise buildings - derived from unexecuted Futurist designs of the 192os - were raised at the entrance. Claimed to be 'the first Socialist street', it was completed in 1960 as Stalin-Allee but renamed Karl-Marx-Allee in 1961 and reverted back to Frankfurter-Allee in late 1991.

Partly in response to this essay in monumentality, of a type outmoded in western Europe and also strikingly different from the grids of drab tenements built for the masses throughout the Communist world, the administration of West Berlin sought to express contrary political and aesthetic ideals. In 1953 architects from fourteen countries, including Walter Gropius and Le Corbusier were invited to design apartment blocks to relieve the housing shortage and also to form a permanent international building exhibition, named the Berlin Interbau (22,5). A site on which only a few habitable buildings had survived, was cleared and laid out in accordance with Le Corbusier's notion of a city as a park with widely spaced high-rise buildings in it surrounded by roads for fast automobile traffic (see p. 837). Housing had priority, but on a bombed area where no-one wanted to live, near the notorious wall rushed up by East Berliners in 1961, three architecturally distinguished public buildings were erected to constitute a Kulturforum: the New National Gallery designed by Mies van der Rohe, a founder of the International Modernism who had emigrated to the USA after the suppression of the Bauhaus, and the Philharmonie concert hall and the State Library by Hans Scharoun (1893-1972), the creator of an individual Expressionist style who had been unable to practice under the Nazis.

Neither the Interbau nor its successor hig arde ubu popular with the public. They seemed hardly less authoritarian than the Karl-Marx-Allee, prescribing how people were to live. Under increasing pressure from residents, the city's senate began to change its urban policy. In 1977 a new international building exhibition, IBA, was initiated for the development of sites smaller than the Interbau, more tightly planned and integrated with the remains of the inner city. At the same time a scheme was launched to fill gaps where small groups of buildings had been demolished and to rehabilitate damaged survivors. Commissions were given to members of the new postmodern generation of architects including Arata Iozaki, Rob Krier (b.8), Charles Moore and Aldo Rossi. The use of traditional materials and colours for exteriors was recommended and a limit set on height, meime foute y Zaha Hadid (b.1950) whose commercial and low-cost apartment block enlivens the junction of two uninteresting streets (22,53). SLICCLS () -11 :n 1080

The demolition of the wall in 1989 opened a wide stretch of no-man's-land for develoment. Aer the e-nitn of Germany in 199o and the subsequent re-establishment of Berlin as capital, the Reichstag building (parliament)which of the western sector was lavishly refurbished with a new dome by the British high-tech architect Norman Foster, completed in 2000. Nearby the residence for the Chancellor and ministerial offices are set in parkland beside the river Sprey. Throughout the former eastern sector buildings are being inserted in the urban fabric, notably Daniel Libeskind's Jewish Museum (22,54). The main focus has been, however, on the site of the totally destroyed Potsdamer Platz, a centre of mondaine life before the war and now a point at which the divided city can be welded together. In 1992 the competition for a master-plan was won by the Italian Renzo Piano (b. 1937) who was also one of the architects of various nationalities commissioned to design individual buildings for diverse functions. Shops, a cinema and theatre, an hotel and highrise office and apartment blocks were financed by Mercedes, Sony and other German corporations whose logos are prominently displayed on them, giving the platz the appearance of a monument to the triumph of post-Cold War capitalism.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/246e44ce5d518cc0804e83ccae3fe6612f3a2a18f6bf28933826de9cc343bfd2.jpg)

Throughout Europe war damaged cities were reconstructed on plans similar to those of the Berlin exhibitions, the same experiment with Le Corbusier's ideal and its rejection in favour of cu clo

2,53 RIGHT Zaha Hadid. IBA housing complex, Stessemannstrasse 109. Berlin, 1994.

of buildings, recommended by the International Congress of Modern Architecture in 1956. The situation was different in the USA where destruction was effected not by bombs but by speculators in league with planning officials bent on so-called urban renewal. An appeal by Jane Jacobs (see p. 865) for the preservation of city life marked the beginning of a reaction against theories of urban design which, since the mid-nineteenth century had been paradoxically anti-urban in their aim to present alternatives to the traditional city in planning concepts she ridiculed as 'the Radiant Garden City Beautiful'. Civic authorities soon began to heed the wishes of residents. With the realization that a city is a living organism that can survive only a limited number of implants, old street systems and individual buildings were less often destroyed. Greater attention has also been paid to the need to reduce

pollution especially by limiting the automobile traffic that had previously been privileged. Ian McHarg (b. 1920), an influential teacher in the USA, has argued forcefully that urban design must take into account all the ecological, climatic and historical features of a site. Grandiose groups of buildings for offices have been master-planned and realized on the periphery of big cities - Canary Wharf, London begun 1988, and La Défence, Paris from 1989. But the emergence of a post-industrial information economy, especially in the USA, has shifted the attention of urban designers to self-contained Edge-City suburbs or 'technoburbs' which connect with each other rather than with the old centre. Closely integrated complexes with a strong neighbourhood identity from which automobile traffic is excluded are envisaged by Peter Anthony Calthorpe (b. 1949) in The Next American Metropolis (1993).

Daniel Libeskind, Jewish Museum.   
Berlin,, 1996.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/226afdb18c1ac6c530b5622b9adef74952964bf3d02b796c6b4a88ceda711746.jpg)

There is no chance to contemplate the historically sequential products of human enterprise in such an environment. Here the very modern co-exists with the natural and primitive, and even the natural is an elaborate contemporary fake. In many respects the KunstHAL is a Post-Modern space, yet it is one that requires its occupants not merely to accept the end of Modernism, but to use it as an opportunity to re-examine their understanding of history and human development, and to reassess their thoughts about how art can speak to current human needs.

In the Jewish Museum, Daniel Libeskind (b. 1946) has created a building whose very form derives from the history of Jews living in Berlin (n,). Situated next to the baroque Berlin Museum, Libeskind's Jewish Museum can be entered only by means of an underground passage from its neighbour. Its zigzag footprint is cut through by a straight line that forms a series of void spaces throughout the building reaching from ground level to roof height, which can be looked into but not entered by the public. The one similar space which can be entered is the Holocaust Tower that stands slightly apart from the main building and can again be reached only at basement level. To one side is a courtyard dedicated to the poet Paul Celan and to the other a garden, named after the writer E.T.A. Hofan enters themeal-cadmusum throughmany, varuslyorientated slits whose random placement is in fact derived from the pattern of lines formed by joining together places of Jewish residence on a map of the city. To enter and move through the building is, then, to engage in the excavation and contemplation of culture and history. A central staircase rises through the centre of the museum, metaphorically extending up towards a future that, far from being pre-formed according to the Utopian ideals of Modernism, remains open and negotiable.

## History Painting

One area in which the freedom from dependence upon a particular view of the development of an artistic medium has been evident at the turn of the millennium is painting. Many painters in the 198os and early 199os were caught within the postmodernist bind that if one made an abstract painting it would necessarily be read in relation to earlier modernist abstraction, and would therefore be seen in large part as a representation of them and not truly abstract at alThe two strands of Gerhard Richter's practice discussed above bear witness to his recognition of this dilemma. One could also see it in the ironic mid-198os use of an Op art style by New York painters Philip Taaffe (b. 1955), Ross Blcner .a Ptr Schuy.958 modernist idea, associated with the critic Clement Greenberg, s tha pi ever t s   t , anguasurace. Inhe cs Te,Ble S o md jy h   a kn   n t variety of abstract elements in the canvases of Jonathan Lsy, arisen out of a meditation on what a painter could effivly oi a ltue tuted wih all kin o imagery once Greenberg's idea had lost its meaning. (4(070) from

Te work   artist such  Lua wens (. u     , particularly in respect of the ong-acceptedistnion reference becomes more interesting than the work. I'd much rather have a reference generate a painting.' Owens uses figuration, drawing, abstract patterning and markmaking, often in such a way that certain marks can be seen as working on more than one level. Her works also draw on aspects of art practice that lie outside painting. In a series of essays examining the relationship between twentiethcentury art and real space, "Inside the White Cube', the writer and artist Brian O'Doherty suggested that exhibitions in the 196os of paintings by artists such as Kenneth Noland and Frank Stella could already be thought of as installations. This was because instead of standing contemplatively before each individual, self-contained work, the spectator needed quite self-consciously to walk around the gallery in order to become acquainted with a number of canvases that had either been executed as a series, or had otherwise been conceived as being closely related in formal terms. For Owens, too, the issue of 'how you, your body, walks past and between paintings' is an important aspect of the aesthetic experience, and her paintings can consequently be understood as having been installed in, rather than simply hung on the walls of a gallery. Commissioned in 1998 to do a work for the Loyola University of Chicago, she worked in situ, making a large untitled painting measuring 10 x 40ft (3 x 12.19m) to hang on the 8oft (24.38m) long gallery wall (). Working on a large scale is something she is familiar with: I've been making big paintings since I was in high school. As a kid growing up in Ohio, I went to the Toledo art museum and the Cleveland Museum of Art, where I saw the big paintings - Frankenthaler, Olitski, Poons, Morris Louis, Rothko. ... I feel that my paintings are very specifically American and have a lot to do with where I come from.' Two areas of horizontal stripes, reminiscent of Kenneth Noland, sit to the bottom left and right of the canvas, their inside edges slanted to leave a central area which, in a manne opposite to the pictorial structure o Louis's 'Unfurled' paintings, broadens towards the base.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/d4ee34f7820d9c31d2b98d4135299795534d7753ce5791429e4be569490b29b0.jpg)  
Laura Owens, Untitled (detail). 998.Acrylic on canvas,  x ft Loyola Uniersity Ch

Areas of color  patches, cloud-shaped blobs, balloon-like circles and smaller spots, some with drawn lines attached that resemble stems, stalks or strings - occupy this central space and the open expanse in the top half of the canvas. The wall of the gallery faces a window that looks out on to Lake Michigan, and Owens's intention to produce a painting that forged a link with the lake outside is accomplished by a line in the stripes which corresponds to the horizon. In ts modeof construction, spatial concption draws on the lessons of Color Field painting and Pop Art, site specific work, installation, and more. Yet none of these factors overrides or obscures Owens's physical pleasure in the manipulation of paint to create form.

The paintings of Neo Rauch (b. 196o) are filled with the evidence of industrial activity, production and civilization. Powerstations, factories, pylons, cranes, workshops, workers and such like appear in strange and fantasy-filled scenarios. In Übung (Practice) two men are at work in a factory yard (2,5). One, in overalls, holds a hose attached to the front of a tanker, while the other, in collar and tie, crouches in the background. A group of what might be petrol pumps stands to the bottom right of the canvas on a distinct green ground while in the background a row of pylons, apparently without any power lines attached, runs oioe istTe  e me is r toe for their surroundings, making the row of tankers look like large toys set out in the playground of the factory yard. The connotation in the painting's title of a school exercise is thus emphasised, especially as the word is written in a large, flowing, youthful script along the bottom edge. The question posed by the composition is what, exactly, the figures are practising for. Everything is out of joint, and if the men are indeed preparing themselves for a life of useful productiveness their efforts look to have been made in vain. The industrial plant is obsolete, disconnected and obviously inadequate to the task of sustaining any real economy. Rauch was born and brought up in Leipzig, and his bizarre, disjunctive compositions, far from being fantastical or other-worldly, refer very directly to his perception of the conditions in the east of present-day Germany. In the recently re-unified country, the eastern part

Neo Rauch,Ubung.1999.Oil on canvas 11sins  t oins (1.8 x 3m).   
Galerie Eigen + Art, Berlin.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/5e9ed5895ee720c67297d7c11165a2178d14e878e89703a47ea9089717e6900d.jpg)  
Ne RauchRanebo0.Oiln pape 8/i  6 (2.65 x 2m). Galerie Eigen + Art, Berlin.

has the character, as one of Rauch's paintings says, of a Randgebiet, a region on the edge of things (n,57). Randgebiet (Marginal Area) shows a couple taking a large dog with a human head for a walk while a vacuum cleaner floats in the foreground and a helicopter hovers overhead. Things are being sanitized, the past is being cleared away, and the pet dog/man is kept firmly on his lead while proceedings are watched from above. The former Communist regime, visible in both the fragments of the military-industrial infrastructure, and the shop-worn Socialist Realist worker-hero stances of the figures, has collapsed, and in its wake, it seems, has come a stark choice: either hang on to a bankrupt and worthless ideology, or gratefully accept the values of the West in its place. Rauch's paintings pick at the wounds of history, ensuring that the scabs of forgetfulness have no chance to form. In their complex narratives we see that neither of these alternatives is adequate: pretending that nothing has changed will achieve nothing, yet to adopt wholesale an entirely new set of values is to obliterate the past, and to reject the past is to deny an identity to all those who, like Rauch himself, grew up and were educated in that society. Another painting from 1998 is ambiguously titled Fang. It could perhaps be translated as 'hunting', but it is also the root element in the word for 'prison' and the verb 'to begin'. All meanings are pertinent.

Another painter whose work mercilessly explores the cultural legacy of his country's past is the Belgian Luc Tuymans (b. 1958). He has said that, "For me, it's impossible to make a joyful painting.' Painting, in other words, cannot and should not be a form of escape from reality. It does not prettify the world, making it acceptable for visual consumption; rather it reveals the harshness and cruelty of things. A child's room is prepared. The bed is yet to be made but there is already a pillow propped carefully against the headboard. A small chest of drawers and a wardrobe stand against the far wall and a table and chair are placed in the middle of the floor. The color scheme involves the pastel shades of blue and pink traditionally associated with childhood. Silent Music (1993) could be an uncontentious image of nurture until we notice that the table in't really sutable or a child, and that anyway the hai is sligh t large in proportion to it. Then the flat plane of the wardrobe door becomes a blank, mute presence, as trag  nl h that falls across the carpet; a shadow whose direction conflicts with that cast by the wardrobe behind. Soon the room comes to seem like a place of confinement  almost of horror  and, since all children's rooms are prepared for them by adults, childhood itself becomes a site of coercion and subjugation. Although far from traumatic, Tuymans's own childhood is something he recalls as unhappy, and in

2,58 Luc Tuymans, Mwana Kitoko, 2000. Oil on canvas, 88 x 35%ins (221 x gocm) Zeno X Gallery, Antwerp.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/0c0a4ff6468f9c6bfeb569c848183c53eb27369089e9485dddd628a16ba9d3a4.jpg)

talking about his work he returns again and again to a language of conflict, describing painting as an act that 'requires a kind of aggression and violence' and stating that 'violence is the only structure underlying my work.'

One recurrent theme in Tuymans's work of the 198os and 199os has been the impact of fascism on European culture in general and on that of his native Belgium in particular. For his exhibition at the 2oo1 Venice Biennale, he turned his attention to questionable aspects of Belgium's own history in making a show entitled 'Mwana Kitoko' (Beautiful White Man). The white man of the painting which gave the show its overall title is King Baudouin of Belgium who made a state visit to what was then the Belgian Congo in 1955, only a few years before it achieved its independence (n,5). Tuymans takes the images for all his paintings from photographs, and in Mwana Kitoko we see Baudouin as he appeared in a documentary film of the visit, walking away from his aircraft steps. Depicted in full length, Baudouin is shown wearing white ceremonial military regalia. He is a distant and iconic figure, less an individual person than the representative of a colonial power. In contrast, Tuymans's portrait of Patrice Lumumba, first prime minister of the newly independent Congo, who was murdered in obscure circumstances prior to Mobutu's assumption of power, is an intimate close-up showing little more than his face. Lumumba's death was explained at the time as the outcome of internecine politics, but Tuymans's series of paintings indicts Belgium and the West as the primary culprits, accusing them of manipulating the political life of the country even as they made a spectacle of relinquishing their control over it. Reconstruction shows several cars, headlights blazing, leaving the scene of the crime (). As the title implies, the picture is an imaginative distillation from the available fragmentary records of what occurred in 1961, and as such it sits in the tradition of great history painting that includes such works as Goya's The Third of May 18o8 (15.8), and Manet's Execution of the Emperor Maximilian (15.41). For, just as it was Manet's intention to accuse France rather than Mexico of being responsible for Maximilian's death, so the threatening colors and disturbing anonymity of Tuymans's painting lead us to reflect upon what really lay behind the assassination. That such questions still need to be asked forty years after the event is emphasised by another painting in the series, Sculpture, in which a Congolese in loincloth and head-dress stands in an Antwerp café. Far from being incongruous and fantastical, the scene is difficult to come to terms with precisely because it is too real, too near the truth. In looking hard at the details of his own country's colonial and imperialist past, Tuymans's painting stands by example as a thoroughgoing critique of Modernism itself. It is all very well for us to celebrate Africa as a major formal and imaginative resource for Modernist art and design, but this is no more than an offensive sham, Tuymans's art tells us, unless we also face up to the responsibilities that such exploitation has placed upon us.
