## Politics and Memory

That contemporary art remains a potent means of unlocking suppressed cultural memory was confirmed by the controversy that surrounded the planning and inauguration of one of the most recent major public art commissions, the Holocaust Memorial designed by the British sculptor Rachel Whiteread (b. 1963). The city of Vienna took the decision in the early 199os that, in acknowledgement of the part played by Austria in World War II, there should be a monument to the 65,ooo Austrian Jews who perished in the Holocaust. The monument was to be placed in Judenplatz, so named because the square was the site of a Synagogue destroyed in a pogrom in the fifteenth century, and in which many Jews had barricaded themselves to die rather than face the choice between forcible conversion to Christianity or execution. Whiteread was announced as the winner of the competition to choose a design early in 1996, but it was not until the autumn of 2000 that the monument was finally unveiled (z2,6o). It takes the form of a single rectangular concrete block that represents the interior space of a library. Rows of small indentations across the surface of the sculpture are the spines of all the books that are lost and cannot be read. Although a set of doors is visible at one end of the block, these, too, are concrete moulds and allow no access to the space which the work occupies. Around its base, the names of the Nazi concentration camps are inscribed in Hebrew, German and English. Set as it is in a largely Baroque square in the heart of Vienna, the square in which Mozart composed Cosi Fan Tutte, Whiteread's subdued monochrome concrete block appears as what the New York Times art critic Michael Kimmelman has described as an 'unsentimental rupture'. It is in the work's ability to break into the pleasant façade of an otherwise cosy existence that its power lies. 'This monument shouldn't be beautiful, it must hurt,' said the Nazi-hunter Simon Wiesenthal during

Original from UNIVERSITY OF VIRGINIA the opening ceremony. The discomfort felt and voiced by those who, for a variety of political, religious, historical and aesthetic reasons, did not want the monument built, the many conversations needed to answer and assuage these anxieties, and the lengthy negotiations over the exact positioning of the work and of how it would relate to the excavated remains of the old Synagogue, were thus part of the process of remembrance and remembering that the sculpture accomplishes. Whiteread has always worked by casting the space around objects rather than the objects themselves. Prior to embarking on the Vienna memorial, her most ambitious project had been House, in which the interior walls of a 3-story terraced house in the East End of London were sprayed with concrete (n,61). The fabric of the house itself was then removed from around this concrete skin to leave a record not so much of the house as of its spaces and of the lives of all those who had occupied them. Since the original house was of a style familiar throughout London, the work spoke to countless thousands who had inhabited similar rooms. It was, though, like the Judenplatz memorial, inextricably linked to its specific location. Speaking of the Holocaust Memorial Whiteread sai:'I felt I was trying to describe a memory, but an onmy Inot u t  aricu vn; there is an awful history to this particular site. [The memorial] was made specifically for the Judenplatz. When I made House it was also absolutely specific to the site. When it was knocked down there were various offers to prolong its life; to put it on wheels and place it somewhere else. I said categorically no; if it couldn't stay there it would have to be destroyed.'

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/4d33ea97681205f4414b2441ed59caf316c8678c29ec95dce63923351a26a292.jpg)  
2,6 Rachel Whiteread, Holocaust Memorial, Judenplatz, Vienna, 2000. Anthony d'Offay Gallery, London.

House excited much debate, both in the media and among the residents of the community in which it was sited. It was only ever conceived as a temporary work, but while there were those who wished to see it demolished immediately, there were others who, as Whiteread says, sought ways to keep it in existence. It quickly became a focus for discussion on a wide variety of issues ranging from homelessness, urban regeneration, and state funding for the arts to domesticity and the character of contemporary family life. These discussions were in part carried on through the graffiti that soon decorated the sides of the work. What both of these public sculptures by Whiteread demonstrated was that contemporary art was not merely a marginal and élitist activity that could be easily dismissed as an irrelevance to the lives of the majority. If they became contentious it was because the themes to which they gave imaginative access were themselves contentious, and it was their success that they helped in the articulation and potential reconciliation of the many conflicting attitudes that circulated around those themes.

Strongly connected to the Modernist view which saw Western society as striving to progress towards the attainment of Utopian ideals, was the understanding that art, insofar as it was able to embody those ideals, had its part to play. Within the hugely extended reach of Capitalist markets since World War II, some critics, notably those associated with the art journal October, have recognised in much art an embrace of the values of what the French Situationist Guy Debord described as the 'society of the spectacle'. In other words, where the work of art once had a use value, it had now been reduced to something with mere exchange value. The pinnacle of this phenomenon was taken to be the Neo-Expressionism of the 198os. As a return to painting after the period of Minimal and Conceptual art it was seen as reactionary, and as a tendency whose success was greatly assisted by the ever-rising prices of works at auction and on the secondary art market, it was thought to be little more than a cynical way to make money. This, however, is a one-sided analysis, and one which sometimes appears pessimistic rather than judiciously critical. The ways in which much of the work mentioned above by Tuymans, Douglas, Whiteread and others makes reference to aspects of history and politics, to the question of gender and identity, be it in ethnic or

Rachel WhitereadUntitledHouse.Comissioneby Artangel, London.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/924209096c215147c81afea663b94320d30dc7dc191c7712abb63b9746c428cf.jpg)

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/36fcc089c47dea88e7d853dc6e1777bfa4745e5fa1293091993305ea9b0af41a.jpg)  
Thomas Hirschhorn, World Airport,gg. Mixed media, installation view at the Venice Biennale, 19gg. Courtesy Stephen Friedman Gallery, London.

individual terms, and to the assumptions and stereotypes that lie behind patterns of social behaviour, resist the idea that the economic imperatives of the global market have reduced all cultural life to just one more kind of consumerism. They show that the decisions we make affect not only those around us in the present; they issue from causes firmly rooted in the past and will have implications that stretch far into the future. The person who views these works, the spectator, cannot help but be aware that he or she is an active participant in the construction of their meanings.

An artist for whom the active engagement of the spectator is seen to be crucial is the Swiss Thomas Hirschhorn (b. 1957). His installation World Airport comprises a vast tinfoil covered table on which sit two rows of model aeroplanes painted in the liveries of different airlines (,62). Each has a fuel line attached to a wing, which passes up to join all the others at a central hub suspended above the middle of the table. The effect is of a silvery spider's web, a network of supply linking all the work's elements together. Around this table-cum-landing strip are a great number of pin boards which Hirschhorn has covered with photocopied articles, photographs, diagrams and books. In keeping with the scale of the installation, the range of subjects covered by these texts and images is equally large, encompassing politics, environmental issues, terrorism, world events, war, philosophy, social policy, and more. Large spoons lie under the table, a reference to Bertolt Brecht's maxim, 'first food, then morals', and, just as we would find in any real airport, there are seating areas where people viewing the work can sit and read some of the many texts in comfort. The amount of material is overwhelming, and while it is clear that one cannot absorb it all, Hirschhorn intends to make us aware that all of it, and more, is relevant to a proper understanding of world affairs and of the workings of what is called the military-industrial complex: 'My work is called World Airport because everything is part of it and it is connected to everything. It could be an aircraft carrier. A separate place, but so large and apparently autonomous as to underline opposing world energies. The civil, worldly,

Digitized by Google clean, neutral, efficient and rational aspects of an airport make it possible to look the other way, to know nothing. In fact, military operations are only carried out in the background in order not to disturb world commerce which envelops the entire world. The airport is calm and yet in an arort t is possble tounderstand many things.s well as the mountain of information provided by Hirschhorn himself there are also several texts by writers invited by the artist to make contributions of their own. Hirschhorn is therefore acting in part as a facilitator, the creator of an environment in which questions can be asked, a range of opinions consulted, and conclusions drawn. And by the same token, spectators become participants, finding it impossible just to look at the work. Instead, they are immersed in its abundance of fragments, and required to determine their own course through its welter of facts and ideas.'Why am I an artist?' Hirschhorn asks. 'Because I take a critical position toward how the world looks and what the human situation is like today. My non-agreement gives me energy to work. I sometimes produce very large works, like World Airport, but they're never monumental. I don't want to intimidate people, so everything's handmade. I want to implicate the viewer not so much in my work as in the issues that my work deals with.'

## GLOBALIZATION

Globalization is very often treated as a largely negative phenomenon, an inexorable process that has led to a homogenised sameness amongst cultural products. This view was strongly contested by the Shanghai-born artist Chen Zhen (1955-200o). 'For me,' he said, globalisation is not homologation but the celebration of differences. It's not a question of loving or not loving; the problem is whether you respect others or not. Art is an evacuation that follows digestion and goes beyond personal taste. Since I started working here I've found an enormous contrast between different works  otherwise I would have gone

back to schools and trends.' His large work Jue Chang - Fit Srokes to Each offers to the viewer the possbiliy mnt qulih Western r Athough he live or the latter prt Western art, Chen Zhen continued to draw on Daoism and Buddhism, as well as Chinese folklore, in his efforts to access those qualities in things that exist beyond their status as consumer goods. 'I'm aware of all currents - from Duchamp etc. I find some artists interesting, but now I am putting together a sort of trans-experience that derives from my own personal experience with other people in different countries.' Since it is impossible for us now to turn the clock back in order to attain a state of affairs prior to the rise of consumerism, what we must do, Chen Zhen's work implies, is to move beyond the present situation to achieve a less strictly controlled and ordered relationship to the world.

Jue Chang, which means something like 'the height of potic perfection' consists of a heavy wooden structure which acts as the support for a large number of bedsteads, chairs and stools. Each piece of furniture has been used as a frame over which Chen Zhen has stretched animal skin to make an idiosyncratic collection of drums. Hooks have also been attached to the structure from which hang various wooden implements that can be taken down and used as drumsticks. Buddhist doctrine states that it is not possible to speak of one's own spiritual being, and that anyone wishing to pray to the Buddha on this subject should be beaten as a reminder that what is really true is ultimately inexpressible. Monica Dematté describes Jue Chang thus: 'Visitors to Chen Zhen's installation are directly involved in the work. Through beating on the hides, they will be awoken by the rhythms of an improvised music that does not obey any pre-established codes and thus communicates with all the force of pre-linguistic intuition and irrationality. The free beating of hides (the ideal substitute for the hides of our enemies and adversaries) serves to exorcise the obscure brutality and violence that is potentially present within all of us.' Jue Chang, as does so much work of the last century, undermines the notion that visual art is something that can be fully comprehended simply by looking at it. Without picking up a stick and hitting the skins, without relinquishing the customary detached, slightly quizzical stance of the gallery-goer, becoming physically engaged in listening to, as much as seeing, what transpires, the viewer remains cut off from the potential of the work.

Just as Neo Rauch investigates through his paintings the consequences of the collapse of Communism and the re-unification of Germany, so many artists from the People's Republic of China have begun in recent years to reconsider the artistic inheritance bequeathed by the Cultural Revolution. One such is Cai Guo-Qiang (b. 1957). A marked aspect of Post-Modernism has been the degree to which the Western art world has striven to become acquainted with and give due recognition to artistic activity occurring in countries beyond Europe and North

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/ba0cd674a37360782f927700d983ddd95b6a100534b26c081b0cc3a69576faf2.jpg)  
Cai Guo-QiangRent Collection Courtyardg.Installation view

America. In the well-established, large international exhibitions such as the five-yearly Documenta in Kassel, Germany, and the Sao Paulo and Venice Biennales, the increased number of artists from China, Egypt, Nigeria, Turkey, Korea, and many other places whose work is included bears witness to this tendency. Alongside this, other, similar international projects have been initiated elsewhere: there are now, for example, biennial exhibitions in Istanbul, Johannesburg, and Kwangjou, Korea. It has remained true, nonetheless, that for a variety of reasons, many of these artists now live and work in the West. Born in Quanzhou, Cai now lives and works in New York, and in his art he explores the dynamics of cultural exchange between his country of birth and the West. In 1995 he staged an action at the Venice Biennale entitled Bringing to Venice What Marco Polo Forgot. Following in Marco Polo's footsteps - the explorer had reached the city of Cai's birth on his travels before returning home - Cai visited Venice, sailing up the Grand Canal in a traditional Chinese fishing he performed acupuncture on the lagoon's famously polu ab returning with so much of present and future material value from the East, Marco Polo had left behind the r.ur  a 9 Cai realised the project Rent Collection Courtyard plc  e , h ognally bee sdu the pe Cultrl Revolution. In the diton  he erroty discovere i he tomb of Emperor Q Shihuangdi i show how much better conditions were under Communist t soldiers and students, were offred as its by Mo Zedong's government to the Communist states of North Korea, North Vietnam and Albania. Cai's new version, on which he was also helped by one of those who had made the original, remained incomplete. Taking over the intention behind the original to show how far conditions had changed over time and turning it to his own purposes, Cai's reworking of Rent Collecting Courtyard reveals once again just how much circumstances have altered in the intervening thirty or so years. Its deliberate state of noncompletion offers the prospect, the hope and the expectation that things will change again in the years to come.

The Iranian artist Shirin Neshat (b. 1957), who has lived in the United States for over twenty years, has made a series of video installations in which the dynamics of gender and power in Islamic society are explored. Turbulent (1998) comprises two videos projected onto opposite walls of a space (2,65). In one a white-shirted male performer stands on a stage facing the camera. Behind him we can see the auditorium and a similarly dressed all-male audience. On the opposite wall a solitary female, black cloaked and with head covered, stands alone in the same hall. The man sings and after he has received his applause, he stands silently while the woman uncovers her face and herself begins to sing passionately and plaintively. The man, in fact, has only lip-synced to a popular song performed by the Iranian singer Shahran Nazeri. He is merely miming the accepted myths and cultural norms of his society. In contrast, the woman sings her own song, and although she is forced by law and convention to perform in an empty auditorium, she is able through the structure of the work to capture the audience of men on the wall opposite her. Neshat says of the work and its theme: '.. men dominate public space and set all codes for social behaviour ... since a woman represents the domestic, personal domain, she carries with her an individuality, which is disruptive for the social order. Therefore, while in public space, a woman must be contained, silent and invisible. In other words, she must be

Shirin Neshat, Turbulent, 98. Video still Barbara Gladstone Gallery, New York.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/3bcaf3a34994522d4474b86897ac5958f557c2b777d3d674cd2c0e71eb820569.jpg)  
Digitized by Google

veiled". The key to Turbulent, however, is that in addition to the Iranian men on the opposite wall, it is we, too, who are caught up in the middle of the work and forced to hear the woman sing. As Neshat points out, the excitement we feel in this situation is not a simple emotion. Nor, in many instances, is it entirely wholesome: 'As concerned as Western media and people are about the repression of the women behind the veil, they relish this! After all, there is something mysterious about a concealed woman - her inaccessibility makes her more desirable, both sexually and intellectually. The more we can't see and know, the more we crave to look and project.' What Turbulent provocatively suggests, then, is the fact that the current concern within Western art and culture to address, understand and come to terms with cultural difference, to give adequate space to alternative points of view, ways of seeing and systems of belief, can very easily become yet one more facet of our allencompassing consumer society. Difference itself can become the subject of spectacle unless we guard carefully against it.

## Spectacle

The very fact of contemporary society's dependence upon spectacle  the importance of the media not simply in reporting, but more significantly in manufacturing those stories, events and personalities upon which it reports - havees this al tl ters h v ly for their own aggrandizement, but more in recognition of the fact that our ideas of what constitutes aesthetic experience at the turn of the millennium must necessarily y n eent    u ha v i been of central concern since the 198os is Jeff Koons (although we should not lose sight of the fact that before him, Warhol's treatment of the media provides the touchstone). Where his earlier work such as the pristine displays of vacuum cleaners (22.8) consciously invoked Duchamp and the readymade as their inspiration and model, Koons's later work shifted its gaze onto Koons himself. Ballon Dog94000) i massively ca-u, red-tinted stainless steel copy of the sort of dog street entertainers make by bending and tying a long, thin balloon. As a dog it returns to his 1992 Puppy, the 39ft x 16ft 6ins x 21ft (12 x 5 x 6.5m) flower-covered Highland Terrier that now sits near the Bilbao Guggenheim, and as a copy of an iatable toy it refes back tRabbit which itself bore echoes of Warhol's helium-filled silver pillows of the 196os. Koons speaks of the large scale of his wok since the ate os thus: ot makig thin to make them big .. these images and these objects are defining themselves that this is the size they want to be. They' archetypes. And archetypes are bigger than any one individual. An archetype is something which helps

Original from UNIVERSITY OF VIRGINIA everybody survive.' If shock or surprise is felt in the face of such work, it is not due to a desire on the artist's part to épater le bourgeois in the old Modernist way, but to the recognition that such attention seeking is a constitutive part of the contemporary expressive and communicative act. As Koons remarked in 1997: 'The public is my readymade.'

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/6c03acfb27870a1d1cc23bd0235a23270b7978f4228d844d1abedd603cc64720.jpg)  
Maurizio Cattelan, Hollywood, 2oo1, Palermo, Sicily. Courtesy Galerie Emmanuel Perrotin, Paris.

Playing upon the public's desire for art that appears to offer a 'good story' is a tactic that has often been used by the Italian Maurizio Cattelan (b. 196o). For Mother (1999), he engaged an Indian fakir to lie buried in earth with only his forearms and hands visible for periods of two hours at a time, and Bidibidobidiboo (1996), picking at the conventional wisdom that we always treat animals better than we treat other people, is a little tableau in which a stuffed squirrel tragically appears to have committed suicide at its kitchen table. In the summer of 2oo1, Cattelan turned his attention to the dense layers of fantasy, myth and folklore that bind together Italy and the United States. That the connection between the two countries is continually being reconfigured and strengthened through the output of the film studios of the West Coast was marked by Cattelan's erection of a replica of the famous Hollywood sign on the slopes of a waste dump outside Palermo in Sicily (n,66). Italy, particularly Sicily and the Mafia, is constantly being picked over for new scraps to feed into the dream factories of the film industry. A decade earlier, Cattelan had had a customized game of tablefootball constructed for AC Fornitore Sud (Southern Suppliers Club) (1991), designed to allow two teams of eleven - rather than the usual one or two - to play each other. When originally shown a contest was staged between the team of the title, made up of immigrants to Italy from Senegal, and a group of players from northern Italy. Though the event was a light-hearted one, it nevertheless brought to attention the economic division between the north and south of Italy, the exploitative use of cheap immigrant labour, and our self-deluding pretence that the camaraderie of sport is able to assuage and transcend such social conflicts and tensions. La Nona Ora (The Ninth Hour)is a life-ike wax model of Pope John-Paul II (,). He li staff stillin his hand, having been knocked over by a meteorite which lies heavily on top of him. A hole in the skylight above indicates where the rock crashed into the gallery, and the glass from the shattered panes lies strewn nearby. The ninth hour - the hour of Christ's death - might lead us to suppose that we are seeing the death of religion itel in this scene, but the Pope actually looks rather unconcerned about what has just happened. He is not vlyhurt, nd will pobaby be le t t u a er we leave the gallery and continue his ministry.There is no simple message in this work, and certainly not one that can be atributed to the artist himsel, since religious bel and the power of the Church's authority are here contradictorily portrayed as both fragile and persistent, endangered and enduring, laughable and utterly serious. Writing of Cattelan and the Thai artist Rirkrit Tiravanija, whose exhibitions in the mid-199os would involve him cooking a meal and offering the food to gallery visitors, the French critic Olivier Zahm said: 'It is clear that ideological forms of protest and opposition are being replaced by practices of infiation that are r harder to identiy. In this cnte he work of art is no longer an object (an integrated supercommodity) or a dematerialized process (the analysis of a system to which it is not subject) but a fluctuating circulation of parasitic information.' And Cattelan himself says, 'In the final analysis I do not invent anything. What I create already exists under our eyes. I simply reorder things by establishing a short-circuit between opposite emotions.'

It remains true, nonetheless, that the art world can be as rapacious and eager to consume the next big thing as any other group of people. The British artist Tracey Emin (b. 1963), whose occasional instances of unconventional behaviour have made her a constant focus of media

M Catte o Mimu

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/eca1ac2dab9c114f5667cc46a5f0602bc63123df5e2d0a862b648c0476232e91.jpg)

22,68 Tracey Emin, My Bed, 1998. Installation, Tate Gallery London. Mixed media, 31×83 × 92ins (79 x 211 x 234cm). Courtesy White Cube, London.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/db76ebb1ddb2155b340bf45758142cae4c320cbdef4542d8b99c5e8fe3655819.jpg)

attention, spoke of this in a 1999 interview. Asked why she could not, perhaps take a year off in order to avoid the endless requests for participation at promotional stunts organized by magazines, posing for famous photographers, attendance at showbiz parties, and so on, she replied: 'A year off from what? Tell me what it is I'm going to take a year off from. This is what I do. This is my territory.' Since her arrival in the art world at the beginning of the 199os with a one person exhibition with the ambitious and somewhat tongue-in-cheek title,'My Major Retrospective', Emin has continued to mine her own experience, incorporating the most intimate details of her life into the drawings, prints, videos, texts, patchwork hangings and installations that make up her art. In the best tabloid style the incidents she brings under public scrutiny are by turns colorful, emotionally charged, sensational, outrageous, tragic and romantic. She can at one moment appear depressed, psychologically fragile, full of self-loathing and at the mercy of those around her, and in the next present herself as composed, self-assured and entirely in control: take me or leave me, but don't expect me to conform to your moral code. Raped when a teenager, abused by stepfathers, her ability to have children taken away through undergoing an abortion, she brings all of this and more into her art. The work excites the worst prurient instincts by offering viewers the prospect of uncomplicated sensation, only to undercut its delivery.

Emin expects her audience to want certain things of her, but refuses to let it take her over because what she has is also extremely personal: 'I was thinking more about the narcissism behind what I do - the self, self, self  and how difficult it is for me to really share things, even though I think I am sharing all the time. In my personal life it causes a hell of a lot of problems. At one point I would have sacrificed everything to be what I consider normal. Like the oher day I hated my art so much I wanted to smash it up, like you abuse a fithful lover. I was so angry with it because I thought this is all I fucking have.' Everyone I Have Ever Slept With 1963-1995 (1995) is a tent on the inside of which Emin has appliquéd the names of all those with we a e . In e  e m one has to crawl into the tent too. Included in the roll call, of course, are all her boyfriends, but there are also the names  inds whoar  be ith nseovers and the like, as well as her twin brother with whom she shared her mother's womb. Sleep, dreams and nightmares were also the subject of My Bed, an installation which comprised her bed complete with rumpled sheets and littered with the detritus of living - dirty knickers, condoms, used tissues, empty bottles, and so on (,6 There are many precursors to this work throughout the history of art, from Yoko Ono and John Lennon's Fluxus sleep-in for peace, through Robert Rauschenberg's Bed, to the divan on which, for example, Goya's Maja reclines, and beyond, but it is also an uncompromisingly contemporary version of the theme. It is that saccharine te the newspapers coyly reer o as a'ove nest as wel as the pace where we see played out all those dependencies and addictive longings to which we are all,in our weakness, prone, but which the famous have to deal with in the gare of a salaciously disapproving public gaze. My Bed taunts the viewer by proffering the old nostrum that art is a safe territory, insulated from the real world, where those fortunate enough to be deemed artists are able to behave in ways that there  the wo cannot, only to laug a suc self-indulgent nonsense.

# Glossary

AbacTe fat slab on top o a capital, be an architrave, arch or lintel.

Abstract art The term has two main applications: 1. Art that is non-representational, purely autonomous and makes no reference to an exterior world, e.g. Suprematism, Abstract Expressionism; 2. Art that 'abstracts' its images from the visible world, e.g. Cubism.

Acadomy A place of study or society to advance the arts or sciences, named after the place Akademia in Athens where Plato taught. (Akademia was named after a legendary Attic hero, Akademos.) The first academy of art, founded in Florence by Giorgio Vasari in 1563, was an association of artists who hoped thereby to promote their status from that of artisans to that of practitioners of a liberal art'. The French royal academy founded in 1648 and the many later academies (e.g. the British Royal Academy, founded in 1768) were also institutions for teaching with facilities for studying the art of the past and the nude model in a life-class.

Acanthus Mediterranean plant with thick, prickly leaves, the supposed source of much foliage ornament as on Corinthian capitals (4,44).

Acropolis The citadel of a Greek city, built at its highest point and containing the chief temples and public buildings, as at Athens.

Acrotoria Small plinths for statues or ornaments placed at the apex and ends of a pediment; also, more loosely, both the plinths and what stands on them.

Acrylic palnt A synthetic medium developed c. 1960, quick-drying and retaining brightness. It permits effects of transparency and impasto but is generally used for work in flat color (21,19).

dobe Mud-brick, sun-dried but not fired.

Adiul A shrine, tormed by two columns supporting an entablature and pediment, enclosing a statue in a classical temple; the term is also used for the similar framing of a door. window or niche with two columns, halfcolumns or pilasters supporting a gable, lintel, etc.

Aerial perspective, see perspective.

g The open space in a Greek or Roman town used as a market-place or general meeting place, surrounded by porticoes as in a forum.

As Part of a church or hall parallel to the main span and divided from it by an arcade of piers or columns or in rare cases by a screen wall.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/2f7bfd8f5234c8a792878969a3f4f5fb04835616180fea0b77a792cc2f81300d.jpg)

Alabaster A fine grained stone of which there are two distinct types. One, a carbonate of lime, was used for carving in ancient Egypt and Mesopotamia (2,2). The other, a sulphate of lime, was much used for small-scale carvings in Europe from the Middle Ages onwards.

Albumen print A photographic print on paper coated with egg-white (albumen) and sensitized with a solution of silver nitrate, in general use 1850-c.1920.

Altarploce A devotional painting or sculpture placed on, above or behind an altar, peculiar to Catholic Europe where it was introduced in the early thirteenth century when priests began to celebrate Mass with their backs to the congregation. Many depict multiple scenes and are on several panels (see polyptych, triptych), hinged so that they can be concealed or revealed as required. See also predella, reredos.

Ambo A stand raised on two or more steps, for the reading of the Epistle and the Gospel, a prominent feature in early medieval Italian churches. Sometimes two were built, one for the Epistle and one for the Gospel, on the south and north sides respectively. After the eleventh century the ambo was replaced by the pulpit.

Ambulatory An aisle surrounding the choir or chancel of a (usually medieval) Catholic church (9.35).

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/5bd78dfb0d57e35a44f109cc23cc553b6c5d008308b96241f7490b4bdb64ac46.jpg)

Amphitheatre An elliptical or circular space surrounded by rising tiers of seats, as used by the Romans for gladiatorial contests (5.44).

Amphor A tall two-handled vessel with an cgshaped body, spreading neck and, usually, a wide foot. Used by the ancient Greeks and Romans for oil, wine, etc.

Anastashs Byzantine painting of Christ's Harrowing of Hell (9.73).

Ang In Chinese roof construction a long transverse bracket arm with the function of a lever. An angdou is placed directly under a root sloping downwards to the eaves.

Ankconk A religious image without any devotional function.

Annular Ring-shaped, as in an annular barrel vault or annular passage.

Antfix An ornamental block used in Classical architecture to conceal the ends of the roof tiles, later used decoratively.

Antefix

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/f188ec6962cdc39a2355b9ceb4eed8bea901ec9dd056d495efd0074df6c34edb.jpg)

Apadana The columned(hypostyle udicnchall o Pr i e Per. see p. 116. T.tmont

Apocalypse The last book of the New Testament. devoted to revelations of the future; hence. a prophetic disclosure or revelation.

Appropriation The copying of images " purposes different from those for which they were originally intended, e. the reproduction of a famous work of art in a commercial advertisement. From the early 1980s appropriation has also been adopted as a means of challenging the premium put on artistic originality. ". Jhi!

Apurs Heavenly maiden of the Buddhist paradises; in Hindu art, a member o Inds seraglio (6,65). mstion of J

Apse vaulted semicircular termma building, usually  church Apsidal, see apse.

Aquatint A print from a metal plate coated with a porous resin on which the design has been 'stopped out', i.e. painted with a resist varnish to prevent it being bitten by acid. The process is repeated to the required degree of complexity, including sometimes the addition of etched lines.

Aquoduct An artificial channel for carrying water, usually an elevated masonry or brick structure supported on arches, invented by the ancient Romans (5,42).

Arbesque Intricate surface decoration of plant forms, spirals, knots, etc. without human figures (see also grotesque).

Arcade A range of arches on piers or columns, either free-standing or blind, i.e. attached to a wall.

Arch The spanning of an opening by means other than that of a lintel. The most primitive form was made by corbelling. True arches are curved and so constructed that the downward thrust of the weight of their own material and/or of that above is converted into outward thrusts resisted by the flanking material. There are various types and forms of arch of which the Basket, Horseshoe, Lancet, Ogee, Stilted and Tudor are notable.

Archak Antiquated or old-fashioned; but when used in connection with Greek art the term refers to that of a specific period c. 600-500 see pp. 129-33.

Architectonk Pertaining to architecture or expressing the spatial and other qualities peculiar to architecture.

Architrave The lowest part of an entablature, see orders.

Archivolt The continuous molding on the face of an arch, also the underside of an arch.

Arcuated A building dependent structurally on arches as distinct from one constructed on the post-and-lintel or trabeated principle.

Armature Framework on which sculpture in clay is supported.

A secco, see fresco.

Ashlar Hewn rectangular blocks of stone laid in regular courses.

Assomblage An art-work composed of threedimensional objects, either natural or manufactured, usually junk.

Asymmetry Without ymmetry.

Atlantos Full-length or half-length male statues used instead of columns, especially by German Baroque architects; also called telamones, See also caryatid.

Atrlum Inner courtyard of Etruscan and ancient Roman house (5.34), also the forecourt of an Early Christian church, see p. 306.

ttl In Classical architecture the story above the main entablature; or more loosely, the upper story of a building if less high than the others; or the space within a sloping roof.

Aurol Light encircling the head or body of a sacred personage, sce also glory, mandorla, nimbus.

Avant garde Literally, the vanguard; more loosely. those in advance or ahead of their time - or thought to be so.

Axial composition One with a central axis and bilateral symmetry.

Adal plan Planned longitudinally or along an axis. See also centrally planned.

An imaginary straight line about which the earth or any other body rotates; or, in art and aitecturenmagay raight  passg centrally through a figure or composition, a façade or ground-plan, so as to give an impression of balance.

Baldacchino Canopy over a throne, altar, etc. (13,15).

Balustrode A series of balusters (short posts or pillars) supporting a rail.

Boptistery A building for Christian baptismal rites containing the font; often separate from the church.

Barrel vault, see vault.

Bast A very hard, durable, dark colored (usually black) stone used for sculpture in ancient Egypt and the Near East, see p. 59.

Basilica An ancient Roman colonnaded hall for public use, later adopted as a building type for Early Christian churches. The Christian basilica had acquired its essential characteristics by the fourth century: oblong plan, longitudinal axis, timber roof either open or concealed by a flat ceiling, and a termination either rectangular or apsidal. It is usually divided into a nave and two or more aisles, the former higher and wider than the latter, lit by clerestory windows and with or without a gallery. See p. 307.

## Basilica

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/a0d93edadbe8970ee62cd55e5eee86e7643d2f2bfdc727f0eeace0d1caeaa8f2.jpg)  
Apse or tribune

Bas-relief, see rlief.

Buttor The inclined face of a wall.

Bay Vertical division of the exterior or interior of a building marked by fenestration, an order, buttresses, units of vaulting, etc.

Bawar Persian word for market (suq in Arabic), usually an irregular conglomeration of alleys, roofed or covered with awnings, lined with shops and entered through gates that can be closed at night. In the early nineteenth century the word bazaar or bazar was adopted in the West for markets for charity, later for shops selling miscellaneous goods.

nday A process of shading by means of small uniform dots, invented by the New York printer Benjamin Day (1838-1916) for cheap photographic reproductions; also imitated by Pop artists, notably Lichtenstein.

Blind arcade, see arcade.

Bodhisatty One capable of attaining Buddhahood but who renounces Nirvand and chooses selfessly to remain on earth to help others.

Bollard A thick post of wood, metal or stone for securing ropes and hawsers, usually on a ship or wharf but also elsewhere; also, by analogy, similarly shaped posts used in series to limit vehicular access to an area, sometimes with intervening chains.

Book of Hours A private prayer-book containing the devotions for the seven canonical hours of the Roman Catholic Church (matins, vespers, etc.), liturgies for local saints and sometimes a calendar. Intended for lay use, they were often elaborately illuminated.

Boss A projection, usually ornamental, covering the intersection of ribs in a vault or ceiling.

Bracket A small supporting piece of stone or other material to carry a projecting weight; often a scroll or volute in form.

Bross An alloy of copper, zinc and often other metals which usually takes on a yellow color when polished. Until the nineteenth century the words brass and bronze were interchangeable, and to the present day works in brass (e.g. Benin sculptures, see pp. 5346) are sometimes erroneously described as bronzes.

Brevlary A prayer-book containing the daily prayers, psalms and hymns generally intended for use by the clergy.

Broken ground Short thick strokes of oil paint applied over a ground to represent shimmering effects of light.

Broken pediment, s peent.

Bronze An alloy, mainly of copper (95% to 99%) and tin, sometimes with small quantities of lead and/or other metals, that can be formed by casting. It was first produced by the second millennium : (Bronze Age) in Mesopotamia and China and, although gradually superseded by iron for most of its initial purposes (tools, weapons, etc.), it remained the metal most often used for sculpture until the twentieth century. The ter bronze is often appld lose to sculpture in brass and copper.

Buddhist railing A barrier of stone carved to resemble a wooden fence, with horizontal slats woven through the verticals, made to surround a stupa (as at Sanchi, see pp. 231-2), but also carved in relief as a symbol.

Burln A tool with a sharp metal point used for engraving, mainly on metal, and epecially in print-making.

Bur A rough ridge of copper on either side of the line made by a needle in drypoint print-making; also the uniformly rough surface of a plate prepared for mezzotint.

Buttress A projecting support built against a wall to give additional strength, usually to counteract the lateral thrust of an arch, vault, etc. A flying buttress is in form an arch or half-arch transmitting the thrust of a vault or roof from the upper part of a wall to an outer support, see p. 398.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/820eee8f585a08a05bd5b8c9873f4d10b674863dd5eba38c61a1c2f25363cb1f.jpg)

Cabinet picture A small picture intended to be examined closely and at leisure, like a precious object or curiosity. The term derives from the seventeenth-eighteenth-century 'cabinet of curiosities' in which precious objects were kept and displayed.

Calligraphy Fine (literally "beautiful") handwriting (8,1).

Calotype The name patented in 1841 by W. H. Fox Talbot for his photographic process by which an image projected on a sheet of sensitized paper is chemically fixed as a negative and may be placed over another sheet of sensitized paper and exposed to light to make a positive print. This was the first negative-positive process, the basis for others by which it was superseded.

Camco A gem, hardstone or shell having two layers of color, the upper of which can be carved in relief and the lower used as a ground (5,58).

Camera obscura The forerunner of the photographic camera: a dark chamber in which images of objects outside are projected on to a wall by light admitted through a pinpoint aperture or lens. Developed in the seventeenth century as a portable box with a mirror to receive the image which would be seen through a ground-glass screen on top, it was occasionally used by artists as an aid for drawing. The invention of photography followed the discovery of light-sensitive chemical substances that could fix such images.

Campanlle Italian for bell-tower, usually one built separate from but near a church.

Cantilever A projecting beam, canopy, etc., supported by a downward force behind a fulcrum. It is usually anchored at one end by the weight of the structure above. Being without external bracing, it appears to be selfsupporting.

Cantilever

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/1771dfcb4875a4fb1a1e2fef36860c9478d89f451aa21b01aef5ae6e1b8cff54.jpg)

Canvs A coarse unbleached cloth woven from hemp or flax, much used in the West as a support for oil painting since the fifteenth century. For this purpose it must be stretched over a wooden armature (called a stretcher) and primed with neutral colored paint to provide an integrated surface.

Capltal The upper member of a column. See orders.

Cardboard A stiff type of thick, compressed paper sometimes used in the West, from the early nineteenth century, as the support for oil paintings, usually small (17,27).

Cartoon A full-size drawing for a painting. (In modern usage, a satirical or comic drawing, especially one in a newspaper.)

Cartouche An ornamental panel, like a scroll or sheet of paper with curling edges.

Caryatid A full- or half-length female statue used as a support instead of a column, see 4,19; if carrying a basket it is called a canephora. The term is also used loosely for male statues serving the same function, see atlantes. See also herm, term.

Caseln Milk protein mixed with pigments for painting.

Casemate A vaulted room built within the thickness of a rampart or other fortification.

Casting A sculptural process often used for the reproduction of a sculpture, medal, etc., the term cast being used primarily in this sense. In casting a liquid medium (e.g. molten bronze, plaster) is poured into a mold. There are various methods, see cire perdue.

Colla The body of a Classical temple as distinct from its portico and colonnades; by extension, the chamber for a cult image in any temple.

Centerlng Wooden framework used in construction of arches and vaults, removed (or 'struck') when the mortar has set.

Contrally Planned A building which radiates from a central point, as distinct from one on an axial plan.

Coramk A nineteenth-century term covering porcelain, faience and all types of pottery.

Chaltya A Sanskrit word for a sacred mound, especially a Buddhist stupa. A chaitya-hall is a building or, more usually, an artificial cave, with an apse enclosing a stupa (6,8; 9). A chaitya-arch is an English term for a recurrent motif in Hindu architecture resembling in miniature the arches (derived from the crosssection of a vaulted wooden building) which crown the entrances to chaitya-halls.

Chalke Wine cup used at Mass; called a Communion Cup in reformed churches.

Chalk Calcium carbonate, a white substance used for drawing, ground to powder and mixed with pigments for pastels, also a component of gesso.

Champleve, see enamel.

Chancel That part of the east end of a church in which the main altar is placed, reserved for the clergy and choir and often separated by a screen (Lat. cancellus, hence the term) from the rest of the church. The term is now often used for the whole continuation of the nave east of the crossing.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/ecb7e576161ed08f976aa0b94f5cc97e321a629ac3c2e9b8649c6c10ebde08e1.jpg)

Chaptor house The place of assembly for the discussion of business in a monastery.

Charcoal Charred wood used for drawing, probably from very ancient times though the first recorded reference is in ancient Rome.

Chashltsu A small Japanese building, or room, for the tea ceremony (12,89).

Chasing The tooling of a metal surface to add decoration s on ilver) or move bi from a cast (e.g. bronze) object.

ChattAn honorifc Indianumbrellacnoy. also one simulated in stone as on the mast of a Buddhist stupa (6,6), in Hindu temple architecture or Indian palace architecture.

Chovet French term for the east end of a church, comprising the cholr, ambulatory, apse and radiating chapels if there are any (9,36).

Chiaroscuro In painting, the manipulation of light and shade to give the effect of modelling.

Chigi Japanese name for the scissors-like finials on the roof of a Shinto temple.

Chlorite or chlorite schist A hard dark green stone used for sculpture in ancient Egypt (2,43).

Cholr That part of a church where divine service is sung, usually part of the chancel. A partition of wood or stone separating the choir and chancel from the nave and transepts is called a choir screen. Often elaborately carved, it corresponds to the iconostasis in a Greek Orthodox church.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/89c14b795002f279dbc8b644e530adefd919179942287214f807f8cc7c1cef7e.jpg)

Chromogenk color print A photographic print made by a process using dyes incorporated in silver elatin emulsion, inventedin the n the 1930s and first perfected by Kodak.

Ciborlum A type of baldacchino, a canopy supported on columns over the altar f church.

Cire perdue Lost wax - a process of making a cast from a model in wax by enclosing it in clay, meltig the wa t dillig te resulting mold with molten metal usually bronze) or plaster. 11 . ∞i

Chrostory Theper stage f te ave w i and pierced with windows (9.43).

Cloisonne, see enamel.

Costr n encose spcesually uro monastery. tes

Cluster per Synonym or compoundr. Lina

Codex A manuscript bound as a book, nngu th f which it superseded inthe fourth cenur

Coffering Decoration of a ceiling, a vaun arch sofft, consisting of sunken square or polygonal panels, as in the Pantheon R (5,49). -iao

Collage A composition made by gluing p (19,26). .ad with a

Collodlon negative A glass plate codteu light-sensitive emulsionof collodion cotton dissolved in alcohol) and other chemicals, inserted in a camera and exposed while still wet, to make a photographic negative. This type of negative was much used from 1850 until the 1870s.

Collotype A process allied to lithography, for printing hotographs by tansferring ages from negatives to plates coated with gelatin, which hardens on exposure to light and absorbs ink, invented in 1855 and soon improved.

Colonnade A row of columns carrying an entablature or arches.

Color The sensation produced on the eye by light rays. The seven main rays that make up the visible spectrum are subdivided into three primary colors or hues - red, yellow and blue - each of which has a complementary color composed of the other two. Color values are determined by the amount of light different hues reflect (yellow has a high, blue a low value), irrespective of the saturation or purity of the hue. See also local color.

Colossal order A Classical order of giant columns rising from the ground through more than one story (11,41).

Colossus A statue very much more than life-size. wal me"oiza.

Column A free-standing upright member of circular section normally intended as a support though sometimes constructed independently as a monument (5,66; 6,1). In Classical architecture it consists of a shaft, capital and, except in Greek Doric, a base, see orders. The twisted column that appears in Baroque architecture was supposed to derive from prototypes in Solomon's temple and is called a solomonic column.

Compound pler A pier with several engaged (sometimes detached) shafts or demi-shafts against its faces, much used in Romanesque and Gothic architecture (9,23; 35).

Compound pier

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/a2e329606babca285f79d1f8eb12f4f84ad92e224d50c67f03d7eec39d8eee96.jpg)

Computer graphlcs Two-dimensional art-works created by computer.

Concrete An ancient Roman invention. Unlike modern concrete, which can be mixed and poured, ancient Roman concrete had to be laid in courses, though a slow-drying mortar was discovered in the time of Augustus. It became a determining factor in the development of Roman architecture, see pp. 199201, but was forgotten and unused for over a thousand years afterwards. Revived in late eighteenthcentury France, it was much developed from the mid-nineteenth century onwards and its use (especially as ferroconcrete) has radically influenced the form of much modern architecture.

Conté crayon A non-greasy stick of powdered graphite and clay with red ochre, soot or blactone, used for drawing.

Contour An outline defining a frm.

Contrapposto A pose in which one part of the body is twisted in an opposite direction to another, see p. 482).

Copper A pure metal that can be formed by hammering or casting. For sculpture it was sometimes used on its own but more often as an alloy in brass and bronze.

Corbelling Masonry courses each built above the one below as a bracket or to form a rough arch, vault or dome, see p. 50 (2,55).

Corinthian Order, ses.

Comke In Classical architecture the top, projecting section of an entablature, also any projecting ornamental molding along the top of a wall, arch etc.

Corpus Latin for body, a word used in several senses, e.g. for an artist's body of works or oeuvre, for the central part of a polyptych, see p. 475.

Cramp or clamp In stone construction, a metal bar or pin used to bind together blocks of the same course. See also dowell.

Crayon Powdered pigment mixed with wax or paraffin and made into a stick for drawing.

Crocket A decorative feature, usually shaped like a curling leaf, projecting at regular intervals from the angles of spires, pinnacles, gables, etc. in Gothic architecture.

Cross One of the most ancient symbolical and ornamental devices, found in many cultures from prehistoric times. In ancient Egypt a form of cross called an ankh, composed of a T surmounted by a loop, was the symbol of life. The swastika, a cross in which the arms are bent at right angles in the same relative direction, is of similarly ancient origin, notably in ancient India (supposedly a solar symbol). A cross became the main Christian symbol when it was adopted by the Roman emperor Constantine in the early fourth century, signifying "Triumph over Death'. Crosses were used as liturgical objects to be set up beside (not on) the altar during services or carried in processions. There were two main types:

1. The Latin Cross, with a long vertical arm and a shorter horizontal one placed more than halfway up. This retained the 'triumphal' significance of early Constantinian crosses and was often elaborately worked and enriched with gold, gems, etc. and termed crux gemmata (9,2).

2. The Greek Cross, with equal arms fixed on a short handle, which signifed the thaumaturgical power of Christ and was used for Benedictions.

Altar crosses - i.e. crosses intended to stand on the altar  were made from the ninth century in the Eastern Church and from the eleventh century in the Western Church. Early in the eleventh century crosses began to be engraved or sculpted, either in relief or in the round, with the figure of Christ crucified, such crosses being termed crucifixes. They gradually replaced the crux gemmata and plain crosses. Altar crosses or crucifixes were abandoned by the Protestant Church after the Reformation and only returned in the nineteenth century. Various types of cross developed as symbols and ornaments include the Maltese Cross with wedge-shaped arms meeting at the centre.

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/2001c94cc6fcba31258cfdf36aca36fe638a4806cf8af48bc41736c5146f98a2.jpg)

Cross-hatching A method of shading in drawings and engravings by superimposing hatching in opposite directions.

Crossing The space at the intersection of the nave, choir and transepts of a church (13,15).

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/3e989d50caf1e19b740347c9b1234de383d9ecde6340d25849582ea302e32acf.jpg)

CrucHfx, see cross.

Cr hkcoss church.

Crux gemmata, see cross.

Crypt A chamber or vault beneath the main floor of a church.

Cun (Ts'un) Chinese term for brush-strokes which indicate modelling and suggest texture within the outline or contour of a form - rocks, mountains, trees, etc., in landscape painting.

Cunolform A script composed of nail-shaped wedges pressed into wet clay, the earliest known form of writing, developed in Sumerian Mesopotamia.

Cupola A dome, sevaulting.

Curtaln wall The outer enclosing wall of a medieval castle or, especially with reference to twentieth-century architecture, a non-loadbearing wall applied to the exterior of a structure.

Cydlopean masonry Masonry composed of very large irregular blocks (2.57).

Dado In Classical architecture, the portion of a pedestal between the base and the cornice; but the word is more often used for the finishing of the lower part of an interior wall, from floor to waist height.

Dagaba or dagoba The name given to a stupa in Sri Lanka (6,48).

Daguorootype Photographic process invented by L.-J.-M. Daguerre (see pp. 671-2) and patented in 1839 for fixing positive images on silver-coated metal plates and widely used. especially for portraits, until the 1860s. Each daguerreotype is unique and the process was superseded by developments from the calotype process by which numerous prints may be made from a single negative.

Dodsks A representation of Christ enthroned in majesty between the Virgin and St John the Baptist, as found in Byzantine art.

Diaper A pattern of small identical nonfigurative, usually geometrical, units adopted either as a means of covering a surface or as a background for figurative work.

Diaper

![](../../A World History of Art (Hugh Honour, John Fleming)/images/693f766ac22c3b09f8ca1d2d14c98c3c91f66e28b5ba6db227362308d3be0d8f.jpg)

Diorite A hard, compact dark colored, black or gray stone used for sculpture, notably in ancient Mesopotamia and Egypt (2,1)

Diptoral tomple A Classical temple with two rows of columns on each side.

.

Dhislonsm The technique of painting with smal areas of unmixed pigments juxtaposed so that they combine optically when seen from a certain distance. Although employed empirically by several artists, notably Watteau, Delacroix and the Impressionists, it was first adopted as a stylistic term by Paul Signac and, independently, by the Italian Post-Impressionists in the 1880s, see pp. 7289.

Dome, see vaulting.

Dork, see orders.

Dougong Chinese term for a cluster of brackets cantilevered out from the top of a column to carry the rafters and overhanging eaves of a roof.

Dowell In stone construction, a wooden or metal pin placed between blocks of different courses to prevent shifting. See also cramp.

Drum A vertical wall supporting a dome or cupola. Also, one of the cylindrical blocks of stone that make up a column.

Drypolnt Technique of print-making by which the copper plate is scratched with a sharply pointed tool, often used in combination with other methods of engraving and etching.

Earth colons Pigments made from materials in the soil, e.g. yellow ochre and umber.

Earthwork A large scale art-work for which the surface of the earth is the medium.

Eased painting A movable painting, usually on wood panel or canvas, as distinct from one on the surface of a wall or ceiling. Fixed altarpieces are also normally excluded from this category.

Edoctie A term applied to works of art and especially nineteenth- and twentieth-century architecture combining elements from two or more historical styles. (The term 'eclecticism' with regard to Bolognese seventeenth-century painting is now obsolete.)

Eboctrum A natural alloy of silver and gold.

Elvation An external face of a building, also a drawing of one made in projection on a vertical plane (11.59).

Embrsure A recess for a window, door, ete., or a small opening in the wall of a fortitied building, usually splayed on the inside.

Emulsion technique A process of painting with mixed liquid pigment in which one of the elements is suspended in minute drops.

Enamdd A vitreous substance that can be fused to a metal surface under heat. Chuumpleve enamel is poured into grooves engraved on metal (9.64). Cloisomme enamel is poured into cloisons or compartments formed by a network of metal bands, the tops of which remain exposed, dividing one color area from another (9.17).

Encaustk An ancient technique of painting with pigmented wax fused with the support by the application of hot irons.

Engaged column or shaft (One that is attached to or sunk into a wall or pier. An engaged portico is similarly one sunk into a façade which continues around and above it.

Engraving An incised design, also a print made from an engraved metal plate (1o,56); sce also etching and woodcut.

Entablature, see orders.

Entis The slight convex curve in the shaft of a column, see p. 141.

Etching A print from a metal plate on which the design has been etched or eaten away by acid (13,34).

Etude French term for a 'study': a painting, more arely of technical abilty but often from the arly nineteenth century regarded as an independent work ofart.It is distinc from a sketc madn the process of creating a larger work.

The architecturally emphasized frot r face of a building.

Facoting The cutting of a round surface into a small flat areas at angles to one another as in a cut diamond; also, in Cubist painting, the representation of forms by small tat planes meeting at sharp angles, see p. 796.

Falonce Pottery with a tin-glaze a glaze composed of oxides of lead and tin combined with silicate of potash) called in Italy mareltea (10,54); also a glassy substance made from powdered quartz in the ancient Near East and Aegean.

Fenestration The arrangement of windows in a building.

Ferroconcrete A modern development of concrete reinforced by the insertion of steel mesh or rods. Also called reinforced concrete.

th An object to which magic power is attributed. The term power figure is nowadays preferred by students of African art.

Figurathe Art Depictions of the visible world. not necessarily including human or animal figures.

Fligree Jewelry made from very fine threads and minute beads usually of gold or silver: by extension, large-scale carved decoration gving a similar effect of intricate delicacy.

Finlal A formal ornament at the top of a canopy. pediment, spire. etc.

Famboyant The French Late Gothic style. characterized by window tracery with long wavy bars of stonework.

Flour do bys Stylized lily with three petals. as in the coat of arms of the French monarchy.

Fluting Shallow concave grooves running vertically on the shaft of a column, pilasteror other surface.

Flylng buttress, see buttress.

Flylng faade The continuation of the façage wall of a building above the roof-line. especially in Mayan architecture.

Font A receptacle for the water used in the Christian rite of baptism (9.29).

Foreshortening Perspective applied form, e.. a foot repented as ponting. not in line with, a pictorial plane ( ...l..

Forum An open space surrounded by purm buildings in an ancient Roman town. us stecentay; si n con agora. .…..ued

Found images, materials or objects in an everyday environment and appropriated for art-works, especially assemblages. . in w ith

Frame construction A type of consuru. - walls and ofprted n  r of wooden or metal beams, the term covers that of both the timber-framed buildings in much vernacular architecture and modern metal and reinforced concrete structures.

Fresco Wall and ceiling painting on fresh (fresco) moist lime plaster with pigments ground in water so that they are absorbed into the plaster. Pigments added after the plaster has dried are said to be applied a secco, see p. 419 (9.80).

Fret A geometrical ornament of horizontal and vertical straight lines repeated to form a band.

Fret

##

Frloe The middle division of an entablature, see orders, also loosely any sculptured or decorated horizontal band.

Functionalism The theory that a building, piece of furniture or other object should be designed primarily to fulfil its material purpose and use and that its form should be determined exclusively by its function.

Fusumaapanese paintings of sliding screens.

Gabl A basically triangular portion of a wall at the end of a pitched roof.

## Gable roof, see hipped roof.

Gallory In church architecture an upper story over an aisle opening on to a nave (9,23), also an exterior feature with continuous open arcading in the upper part of the building. In domestic architecture a long room, sometimes used for the display of paintings, sculpture, etc. (hence the modern term 'art gallery').

Gargoyle A waterspout projecting from a roof or the parapet of a wall carved into a grotesque human or animal figure.

Genre painting A picture of everyday life (13.47).   
Go Thevarius categoris of painting e. history, landscape, portrait, still life etc.

Gasso A composition of gypsum or chalk and size sometimes with other materials. Applied to a panel it provides a smooth absorbent white ground for painting, usually in tempera. It can also be modelled in relief. The term gesso is sometimes used for plaster of Paris, a white gypsum powder that forms a paste when mixed with water and hardens into a solid, used by sculptors for molds and casts.

Gilding Coating with gold, gold leaf or a gold colored substance. In medieval Europe panel paintings were often gilded, see gold ground. Similarly sculptures might be partly or wholly gilded. Various mechanical or chemical methods were used, notably for ceramics, metalwork and woodwork (e.g. for furniture). Today gold is usually applied to metalwork by electrolysis.

Glaz On pottery a glossy waterproof surface produced by the vitrification of silica (present in most clays) with a flux, e.g. oxide of lead, tin, etc. In painting a transparent film of oil paint which modities the solid color over which it is applied.

Glory A circular, oval or irregular flame-like area suggesting light surrounding the head or whole body of a sacred figure, e.g. the Buddha (6,89) or Christ and the Christian saints (9.3o), also called a nimbus or aureole.

Gold The most precious of metals, found in various parts of the world either as veins in different types of rock, or alloyed with silver in electrum or as flakes, dust or nuggets. Its rarity combined with its unique ductility (a single grain may be drawn into a wire 50oft, 150m, long), malleability (it can be beaten very thin, see gold leaf) and permanence (it will not oxidize or tarnish and is not corroded by naturally occurring substances) account for the great value placed on it and for the mystical properties attributed to it - hence its use on Buddhist images and Christian liturgical objects. In its pure state it is too soft and heavy for easy use so is usually alloyed with other metals (pure gold is of 24 carats, a half-andhalf alloy is of 12 carats, the finest alloys nowadays being 18 or 22 carats). The color varies according to the alloy, copper giving a reddish, silver a pale greenish tinge while platinum makes it silvery. See also gilding.

Gold ground Medieval gilding technique used for the backgrounds of panel paintings. Gold leaf is applied to the gesso-coated panel with a glue sizing, usually a dull red in color.

Gold loaf Gold beaten very thin (down to three millionths of an inch, 0.000076mm).

Golden section or mean An irrational proportion, probably known to the ancient Greeks and much taken up by Renaissance art theorists. It may be defined as a line cut in such a way that the smaller section is to the greater as the greater is to the whole. This cannot be worked out mathematically, hence its fascination. Approximately it would be 5:8.

Gopur Monumental gateway to a Hindu temple.   
Gouache Opaque watercolor paint.

Graphk arts The arts which depend on drawing rather than color but generally including all forms of print-making.

Graphic dosign The design of images and/or let usually for commercial purposes.

Graphite A form of carbon, the 'lead" in a pencil.   
Greek cross, see cross.

Grisallke Painting in tones of gray, sometimes suggesting low relief.

Groin, see vault.

Grotesque Fanciful decoration similar to but distinct from arabesque in that it includes human figures, animals and architectural elements.

Ground The surface on which a painting is executed.

Ground plan, sec plan.

Guilloche An ornamental pattern of curving interlaced bands resembling a plait of ribbons.

Gum print A photographic print on paper coated with light-sensitive gum arabic. In the process of fixing it tonalities may be controlled, detail removed, ete., ditferentiating prints made from the same negative. Gum prints were first exhibited in 1858 and were much favoured by art-photographers in the late nincteenth century.

Guttae, see orders.

Halo A glory, usually restricted to the circular area behind a head.

Happening An unscripted performance, usually in an ordinary open space (e.g. street or parking lot) in which one or more artists, using materials without fine art associations, encourage the spontaneous participation of spectators who condition its development; sometimes recorded by photography.

Hatching Parallel lines indicating shadow in a drawing, engraving etc.

Haunch The part of an arch roughly midway between the springing line and the crown, where the lateral thrust is strongest.

Horm A rectangular shaft terminating in a human head. See also term.

Hexastyle A portico or other structure with six frontal columns.

Horogtyphs The signs used in ancient Egyptian picture writing, representing either complete words or syllables forming part of a word.

Highllght In a painting, a spot of the highest or lightest value, usually white.

Hipped roof A roof with all its surfaces sloped, as distinct from a pitched or gable roof which has vertical walls on two sides.
